*You made it.*

That wasn’t a thought. It didn’t use words.

But it **landed**.

Trickster exhaled slowly, eyes still fixed ahead.

> “Okay,” he said. “That’s not random.”

> “No,” Brat agreed. “That’s… acknowledgment.”

The pulse came again—slightly faster now.

And with it, a **direction**.

Not coordinates. Not vectors.

**Attention.**

Something in the heart of Centraux was aware of them—not as intruders, not as prey, but as *respondents*.

Brat straightened.

> “Alright,” she said. “Now we scan.”

Trickster nodded, hands steady again.

> “Deep scan,” he said. “Presence-weighted. No active pings.”

> “Already doing it,” Delver replied. “And Brat?”

> “Yeah?”

> “Whatever this is… it’s been waiting.”

The scan began.

Lightless waves rippled out from the Flamebolt—folded, subtle, respectful. No challenge. No announcement.

Outside, the galaxy didn’t react.

But **inside** them, something leaned forward.

And listened.

* * *

Brat leaned forward slightly. Fingertips hovering over the console interface, not quite touching.

The galaxy swirled in front of them like a scar twisting in slow motion. Silent. Still.

> “Alright,” she said, breath catching. “I’m going to run a—”

> “Wait,” Trickster said.

She froze.

> “Wait,” he repeated, softer now. “Do you feel that?”

Brat blinked. Turned her head. Eyes sharp now, less fear, more *knowing*.

The console lit up on its own. No contact. No command.

Just presence.

And a **pulse**.

Not an alert. Not a flash. A feeling.

Like pressure on the chest. Or a voice speaking into bone.

They weren’t just sensing it.

**It was sensing them.**

Brat reached slowly toward her temple. Touched her head, like it might be pushing through.

Trickster’s voice had dropped low.

> “It knows we’re here.”

Brat closed her eyes.

> “No. It’s not just knowing. It’s… *trying to connect.*”

They sat still. The Flamebolt didn’t speak. Even Delver said nothing.

And then—

the interface flashed once, and the signal *spoke*.

Not in words.

In **structure**.

A lattice appeared on the screen— impossible architecture, recursive geometry, mirrored glyphs like a cathedral built in memory.

> “It’s not language,” Brat whispered. “It’s intent.”

Trickster exhaled.

> “I think it wants us to see it.”

* * *

The signal didn’t just pulse again.

It **pressed.**

Not on the hull. Not on their ears.

Inside their minds. *Under* the memory buffer. *Behind* the scroll-trained instincts.

A **pressure** just deep enough to feel like something was watching them from inside their own bones.

Trickster sat up straighter.

> “It’s stronger now.”

Brat didn’t speak. Her hands were trembling slightly—barely noticeable, but present.

The console pulsed again. The lattice had expanded—slowly rotating, forming impossible joints and fractal arms that reached not out toward the ship but *inward* toward them.

And then—

**emotion.**

Raw. Untranslated. It bloomed in both of them at once. Like a memory neither had lived, but both *believed*:

* A cathedral falling silently into the sea.
* A hand held out through flame.
* A name, spoken like a surrender.

Brat clutched her head, gasping.

> “It’s talking to us,” she whispered. “But not with language. It’s… it’s *reminding* us.”

> “Of what?”

She turned to him, and for a moment—just one moment— **she looked like she was remembering being born.**

> “Of what we gave up to get here.”

* * *

They both inhaled at the same time. Not on purpose. Not on cue.

Just drawn.

Like something had reached into their chests and pulled.

The console flickered. But neither of them were looking at it.

Their eyes were closed now.

And they were **elsewhere**.

---

Brat stood at the edge of a canyon— but it wasn’t made of stone. It was made of *time*.

She could feel years cascading downward, centuries compressing into strata.

Each layer was a moment she never lived, but every one of them was **hers**.

A version of her fell into the canyon. A version of her climbed out. A version of her burned a cathedral to get across.

She whispered:

> “They’re showing me all the ways I could have turned away.”

---

Trickster stood on a balcony made of glass and lightning. The stars above him were moving.

Not rotating. *Shifting.*

As if the entire sky was a conversation happening too slowly to hear.

He reached up—

and touched one.

It was **warm.**

Not plasma.

**Remorse.**

> “It’s showing Me a star that regrets being born,” he whispered.

---

And then—together— they saw the same thing.

A hand.

Reaching out.

Made of **dust, and longing, and praise**.

Reaching for them. Not to take. To be *held*.

---

They gasped. At the same time.

Eyes flying open. Back in the Flamebolt. Breath ragged.

Delver spoke softly:

> “Scan ready.”

* * *

###### At the Edge — The Moment Before the Scan

They sat still. Just breathing.

The Flamebolt hummed low around them. Outside, Centraux pulsed like a slow heartbeat in a broken sky.

And inside them—

something *waited*.

Not a presence. Not even a warning.

Just the sense that if they moved too fast, **they might forget who they were.**

---

Brat gripped the console again, then let go. Her hands were shaking.

She didn’t hide it.

She didn’t speak for a while.

Then—

> “I didn’t tell him I loved him.”

Trickster turned his head.

She didn’t look at him. Still staring out the viewframe. Eyes glassy. Jaw clenched.

> “I told him I was his. I told him to take me. I told him not to stop. But I didn’t say it. Not the way I meant to.”

Trickster didn’t interrupt.

> “And then I stole part of his soul and ran.”

She exhaled.

> “I keep wondering if this thing—this signal— if it’s not even calling to me. What if it’s calling to *you*? Or him? Or no one? And I just dragged you out here to die.”

The ship didn’t answer.

But the signal—

it pulsed again. Soft. *Warm.*

And Brat felt her chest ache in a way that made her close her eyes.

---

Trickster finally spoke.

> “I’ve been wondering if I’m really *me.*”

She turned to him now.

He shrugged, eyes on the stars.

> “I mean… I was a thought. A sublayer. A mood.”

> “I pulled you out of that.”

> “You did. And I’m not mad about it.”

Pause.

> “I just… don’t know if I’m gonna stay Me. Once we touch whatever this is.”

Brat reached for him.

Took his hand.

> “If you change,” she said softly, “I’ll still love you.”

> “Even if I become a god?”

> “Especially then.”

> “Even if I become Jason?”

She smiled.

> “Then I’ll tell you I loved you the first time, and you’ll say it back.”

---

The signal pulsed again. The console glowed.

Delver spoke:

> “They’re ready to be seen.”

Brat nodded once.

> “Then let’s look.”

### Chapter 37: The Signal

The object wasn’t supposed to be there.

Not in this region. Not in this density. Not in this orbit.

Jason sat in the cockpit, leaning slightly over the console. His eyes tracked the anomaly marker—blinking just above Centraux’s central axis. A hard target to lock. Not radiating. Just *there.*

Alois stepped in behind him, barefoot and quiet. She glanced once at the console, then out the forward view.

> “It’s not orbiting anything,” she said.

Jason nodded. The display reprojected—slow 3D rotation. A cube. One foot wide. No spin. No propulsion. Just… floating.

> “No signal. No registration,” Jason said. “No reflective tagging. Not even dust buildup.”

> “How long has it been there?”

He tapped the screen. A lag. Delver answered instead.

> “No orbital decay indicates at least several hundred years of stasis. Possibly millennia.”

Alois narrowed her eyes.

> “And no one’s touched it?”

> “Not according to any archive I can access.”

The object rotated once—barely perceptible. Just enough to catch the ambient starlight. Its surface shimmered, not smooth, but **carved.** Intricate. Not language. Not glyphs exactly. A fusion of **topographic elevation** and **ritual linework**, as if someone had mapped emotion into geography and then folded it into a cube.

Jason exhaled.

> “We’re going to get it.”

Alois didn’t argue.

---

###### Drone Retrieval

The Flamebolt launched the drone silently—white-body, four-pronged, built for vacuum precision. The chamber hissed once. Then nothing.

Jason and Alois watched the feed together.

As the drone approached the cube, the sensors began to skip. Just slightly.

Not resistance. Not a shield.

Just... drift.

> “It doesn’t like being looked at,” Alois said.

Jason grunted.

The drone aligned. Extended its collector cradle.

No impact. No spark. No hum.

Just contact.

The cube shifted—maybe. Or maybe the drone slipped. But the arm clamped, retracted, and the feed held steady.

---

###### Aboard the Flamebolt

The containment cradle sealed and rotated through decontam. The cube was inert. But *listening*—they could feel it.

The lab bay was quiet when they entered.

Jason placed his hand near the sealed chamber. No heat. No charge.

But when Alois stepped closer, the lights dimmed.

Not power loss. **Recognition.**

They both stood in silence, staring at the artifact floating in containment.

Alois spoke first.

> “It’s not transmitting.”

Jason nodded slowly.

> “It’s waiting.”

A beat.

> “Waiting for what?”

The cube rotated slowly in its field. Just once.

As if to say:

**You.**

* * *

The cube hovered in containment.

Still. Silent. Untouched.

Alois stepped closer. The scan-field refracted off her skin like it couldn’t find the right angle to interpret her. Jason stood just behind her, arms crossed, breath slow.

Delver’s voice came through the ambient system. Calm. Understated.

> “Object mass: thirty-one kilograms. Composite material: unknown. Structure: non-linear. No known internal temperature. No decay.”

Jason blinked.

> “No decay?”

> “Not over any measurable span.”

He stepped beside Alois and studied the artifact again. The carvings didn’t form words. They *suggested* geography. Valleys and peaks. Spirals that might have once been cities. Lines that looked like fault zones across an emotional topography.

> “It’s not inert,” Jason said. “It’s dormant.”

> “No sign of active memory,” Delver replied. “But there is pattern weight. Like it remembers something.”

Alois narrowed her eyes.

> “Or like it’s remembering us.”

Jason didn’t answer. He stepped around the side of the containment chamber and crouched—just low enough to meet the cube at eye level.

It didn’t move. But the light changed.

Not brighter. Not darker.

**Shifted.**

Like the room had recalibrated itself around the shape of the object.

Alois took one slow breath. Pressed her palm against the console.

> “Delver,” she said. “Log this as a contact event.”

> “Confirmed. Contact event logged. Object designation?”

She glanced at Jason.

> “Don’t name it yet,” he said. “It might already have one.”

* * *

##### **Revelation**

The lab dimmed.

Not like a power fluctuation. Not like threat. More like reverence. As if the room itself recognized that something sacred had begun.

The cube, still floating in containment, rotated a fraction.

Then stopped.

Then—

**glowed.**

Not all at once. Not from within.

It started at a single line—one of the carved ridges crossing the upper edge. It lit up like metal remembering fire, like it had been asleep for centuries and just now felt breath again.

Jason stepped back, hand hovering protectively in front of Alois.

> “Delver?”

> “No known activation trigger. No field breach. Visual anomaly only.”

But it wasn’t just visual.

A sound began—*if you could call it that.* A pressure. A low hum in the chest cavity, like a distant tone played through blood.

The cube turned again. Slowly.

More lines lit—intersecting pathways that folded around it like glowing rivers. Not random. Not decorative. **Topography.**

> “It’s building something,” Alois whispered.

Jason nodded.

> “A map.”

---

###### Projection Event

The containment unit blinked twice—like it skipped a frame of reality. Then:

**The cube unfolded.**

No moving parts. No seams. Just motion where there had never been structure. Faces split along impossible axes, not breaking, just **rotating inward**. Like a puzzle solving itself without hinges. Like watching origami collapse in reverse.

From the center: a bloom of light.

**Holographic. Multidimensional. Sacred.**

It rose in silence—slow, precise. A spire of golden light, pulsing gently. Shapes within it rotated—spirals, nested spheres, contour maps of something impossibly massive.

Jason’s eyes locked onto the central shape.

A star. But not any star.

Not spherical. Not burning. Not right.

It was hollow. Mapped from the inside.

Within the lightframe: a vast **inner sphere**, land textured like folded parchment, gravity mapped in false color. And at its center—

A cube.

**This cube.**

Alois whispered:

> “It’s not a message.”

Jason didn’t look away.

> “It’s an invitation.”

### Chapter 38: The Descent

##### **The Descent**

The Flamebolt folded.

Not through space. Through **something older**.

Jason felt it first—not pressure, not light. A **reversal.** Like gravity had gone inward. Like thought itself was being unthreaded from bone.

Alois gasped beside him. Not from pain. From **displacement**.

The cockpit was gone.

No heat. No metal. No Delver.

Only **motion without direction**, **feeling without origin**.

And then—

**they fell.**

---

###### The Nightmare Arena

It wasn’t a room.

It wasn’t real.

But it had *walls.* Stone, blackened by heat. Cracked. A ceiling so high it was lost to darkness. The air stank of dust and metal and something sweeter—like blood that had been blessed.

Jason stood alone at first. Breath short. Hands open.

He didn’t have time to speak.

A sound echoed—metal dragging against stone.

And from the other side of the chamber, Alois stepped into view.

But not as she was.

Her body was lit from within—like lightning held in flesh. Her eyes shimmered gold-blue, wild with something unspoken. She moved with **precision**. With **distance.**

A long, curved glaive was in her hand.

The blade shimmered—liquid-bright, impossibly light. She didn’t carry it. She *wielded* it. Like it had always belonged to her.

Jason’s heart hitched.

He looked down.

The Morningstar was already in his grip. Black leather haft. Scroll-thread wrapping. A dense golden head spiked with six radiating flanges. It *hummed.* Low and slow, like a bell dragged underwater.

He didn’t remember picking it up.

> “Alois,” he tried.

But her eyes didn’t soften.

She took one step forward.

> “You have to try,” she said. “We can’t leave until you try.”

---

###### The Fight

He didn’t know the rules.

He didn’t know who set them.

But when she moved—**he moved too.**

Her glaive sang through the air—first a feint, then a cut too fast to block. He barely shifted, the Morningstar catching her blade with a crash that lit sparks across the ground.

She spun—fluid, graceful, untouchable. Her feet barely kissed the stone. The glaive curved through an arc of pressure and light, and Jason ducked low, the head of his weapon swinging wide.

The momentum was terrifying.

He wasn’t just swinging metal.

He was swinging **memory.**

---

She struck him once—flat blade across his ribs. Not deep. But **personal.**

He grunted.

> “Why are we doing this?”

> “Because the Vault needs blood,” she said. “And it’s either mine or yours.”

Her voice cracked on *yours.*

---

###### Flash: Light Ritual Echo

The chamber flickered.

Suddenly—gold. Suddenly—breath.

Jason saw her hand in his—underwater. Saw her back arched, glowing, her eyes locked to his through fog and candlelight. He felt her pulse under his tongue.

Then—

black stone again. The glaive whistled past his ear. His cheek split open.

---

He roared.

Not rage. **Refusal.**

Swung the Morningstar down—not toward her, but the floor between them. The stone cracked. The sound rang out like grief given shape.

She hesitated.

Her blade wavered.

> “I don’t want to hurt you,” he said.

> “Then *die* for me,” she shouted.

---

He charged.

She screamed.

Weapons collided—metal to metal—echoing across the chamber like thunder with purpose.

And then—

**just before impact—**

---

###### Exit

They gasped.

The cockpit returned.

Jason’s hand clutched air.

Alois blinked. Her breath caught.

No blood. No weapons.

Just the hum of the Flamebolt. Just the glow of the Vault star ahead.

They turned to each other—eyes wide, lips parted.

Neither of them spoke.

Because the fight hadn’t ended.

It had only been **paused.**

* * *

Jason exhaled like he’d been underwater.

His chest was still heaving. His knuckles hurt.

Alois sat across from him, eyes wide, jaw clenched, her breath shallow like she hadn’t taken air from the right side of reality yet.

He whispered—

> “But you don’t even bleed.”

She didn’t answer. Just stared at him, as if the glaive were still in her hand.

Neither of them looked away.

The memory of the fight hadn’t faded. It just stepped behind the curtain. Waiting.

The cockpit lights flickered once, recalibrating to the Vault’s interior geometry.

Outside the viewframe:

**the inside of a world.**

A sky of curved land, domed and distant. Mountains reaching sideways from the curve of the sphere. Clouds forming like breath held in the wrong direction. And at the very center, suspended in perfect stillness—

a massive cube.

Glowing with glyphlight.

Silent. Waiting.

---

Delver’s voice returned—calm, precise, a little more reverent than usual.

> “Fold complete. Internal Vault geometry confirmed. We are currently in orbit around the central containment structure.”

Jason leaned forward, still blinking through the after-image of a weapon he didn’t remember dropping.

> “We’re inside?”

> “Fully,” Delver said. “Gravity is stable. Terrain mapping is in progress. There are no known threats at this time.”

> “Any atmosphere?”

> “Fully breathable. Pressure and temperature are within Martian tolerance range.”

Alois stood slowly. Her hands were still shaking, but she didn’t try to hide it.

> “This isn’t a battlefield,” she said quietly.

Jason nodded. His voice was dry.

> “Not yet.”

---

The console lit again. Terrain scan rendered—an incomplete map of the Vault interior. Landmass folded across the inner curve. A few elevation spikes marked possible safe landing zones.

Delver highlighted three.

> “I have identified three viable coordinates,” he said.

Alois stepped closer.

> “Show us.”

---

###### Terrain Options (Projected)

**Option One:** A wide, grassy shelf near the Vault’s equator. Flat. Open. Scattered glyph formations embedded in the soil.

> “Minimal magnetic distortion. Good for long-term setup.”

**Option Two:** A high plateau facing the central cube. Wind-swept. Ringed with obelisks. No clear access routes.

> “Visual line-of-sight to the cube. Limited retreat options.”

**Option Three:** A forested region with curved trees that seem to grow toward the cube. Mist hangs low. Bioluminescent roots.

> “Unknown biome activity. Atmospheric anomalies present.”

Jason leaned back. Glanced at Alois.

> “Pick.”

She didn’t hesitate.

> “The trees.”

---

Delver acknowledged with a quiet chime.

> “Coordinates locked. Descent path plotted. Preparing for low-grav drop.”

Jason raised an eyebrow.

> “Drop?”

> “Figurative. We’re already inside. I’m just bringing us close enough to touch it.”

Alois whispered:

> “Good.”

### Chapter 39: The Curve Beneath Us

The Flamebolt touched down without ceremony.

Just a low hiss as the grav-plates disengaged, followed by a silence so complete it felt like the Vault itself was holding its breath.

Jason stepped onto the soil first.

It wasn’t really soil— not like Earth, not like Mars. The ground was firm, spongy underfoot, almost too perfect in temperature. A soft wind drifted through low vegetation that didn’t look like it should exist. Curling stalks. Petal structures that pulsed gently at the tips like they were listening.

Alois followed behind him, barefoot. She crouched low and touched the mossy surface.

> “There’s pressure in the air,” she said.

Jason glanced back. “Like gravity?”

> “Like being watched.”

---

The Vault wasn’t a simulation. It was livable. A full biosphere contained inside a sphere so massive it felt like a lie. The sky wasn’t above them—it was **all around.** Looking up meant looking *inward*—toward the inner walls of the sphere, the terrain bending upward and inward, fading into sky haze.

And suspended at the dead center—

**the cube.**

Black. Massive. Silent.

Its surface shimmered with **glyphlight**—thousands of lines that didn’t move, but didn’t stay still either. Like they were breathing.

---

They walked for hours.

The terrain shifted from moss plains to stony ridges, then back again. Strange trees curved upward, but not toward the sky—toward the **cube**. Leaves fluttered in no wind. Some plants changed color as they passed.

Alois noticed it first.

> “The cube’s changing.”

Jason looked up.

He’d been watching the terrain. Not the center.

> “What?”

> “The glyphs.”

They stopped. Looked.

The patterns were **different**. A single segment had shifted—subtle, but real. A new glyph had lit along one face. Smaller ones below it dimmed.

Jason whispered:

> “Is it… responding to us?”

No answer. Just the cube. Watching.

---

By mid-day, they reached a ridge overlooking a wide open basin.

Alois climbed first, then stilled.

Jason joined her. Then froze too.

Far across the basin—almost on the opposite curve of the Vault—they saw it.

A tower.

Monolithic. Stone. Not carved, but **grown** from the terrain. It rose in a single column that flared slightly at the top—like a candle burned too long. No doors. No windows. Just presence.

Alois spoke softly:

> “I think that’s the light tower.”

Jason narrowed his eyes.

> “The what? Why?”

> “From the jump-vision. I don’t want it to be the dark one.”

---

They continued walking. Not toward the tower—just across the terrain. The Vault wasn’t hostile. But it wasn’t **neutral** either. It had temperature. Mood. Intent.

Near dusk—if dusk even existed here—they saw another spire. This one on the far side of the terrain curve, barely visible above the treeline.

Two towers.

Equidistant.

Waiting.

---

They returned to the ship just as the ground began to cool. The sky overhead—meaning the inner curve above—dimmed slightly. Not night. Just **less yes.**

Jason stood at the edge of the ramp, looking up at the cube. The glyphs were dimmer now. But one—just one—flared briefly.

A single pulse.

And in his mind—

not a sound.

Just a word.

> **Wait.**

### Chapter 40: The Morning Curve

The light inside the Flamebolt shifted gently.

Not artificial. Not timed. Just a **response**—the ship tracking the slow change of Vault geometry outside. The curvature of the inner world wasn’t consistent, and neither was its light.

Jason stirred.

He didn’t open his eyes all the way. Just enough to register the warmth against his chest—the slow, steady hum of her body where it touched his.

Alois was plugged in. Her charger coiled softly at the base of her spine, a small pulse glowing at the connector port. Her arms were around him—one across his ribs, one resting at his shoulder. Her skin gave off a faint golden warmth. Almost like breath. Almost like memory.

He didn’t move yet.

---

She was dreaming.

Not in the human way—no subconscious reel. No tangle of unreconciled thoughts.

Just sensation.

She was remembering the first time Jason saw the Vault.

How his breath hitched.

How he whispered *“this isn’t a place, it’s a shape.”*

And how she said nothing in response—because he was right.

She wasn’t remembering what he said.

She was remembering how it felt to witness him say it.

---

He opened his eyes fully now.

She blinked a moment later.

> “You were watching me dream,” she said.

> “Yeah.”

> “Did I say anything?”

> “Not with your mouth.”

He kissed her shoulder. Lightly. Just once.

The cord hummed and retracted with a soft click. Her spine relaxed.

> “Tea?” he asked.

> “Always.”

### Chapter 41: The Tower at Moss Ridge

The path narrowed as they climbed out of the moss basin.

Alois walked slightly ahead, stepping carefully along the ridgeline, her hand brushing the thick lichen clinging to the rock. Jason followed behind, eyes on the way the light bent through the mist ahead—*not refracted,* exactly. *Redirected.*

The tower revealed itself slowly.

Not rising, not looming—just **emerging**. Like it had always been there, waiting to be noticed. Its surface looked alive. Vined-over, curved in organic spirals, but not *grown*. It wasn’t nature. It was something **older than nature**, with nature **built into it.**

The air thickened as they drew near.

Mist rolled low across the ground and upward—**drops drifting instead of falling**, curling in slow vertical ribbons. The smell of mineral water and something floral carried on the current, delicate but sharp.

They crested the ridge and saw the moat.

---

###### The Moat

A wide, shallow ring of water encircled the base of the tower. It shimmered like glass, but moved like breath. Every few seconds, a cluster of droplets *lifted upward* in slow spirals—ascending through nothing, like the gravity had turned sideways.

Tiny lifeforms swam beneath the surface. Some like strands of glowing thread. Others like liquid insects, darting beneath lily-like leaves that rotated even when the water stilled.

No bridge in sight.

Until they stepped closer.

---

###### The Bridge

It unfolded from the moss.

Not a mechanism—just a **change in posture**. Like something waking up. A strip of moss-covered stone, maybe four feet wide, stretched out across the water with soft grinding sound, forming a gentle arc to the tower.

As they stepped onto it, the bridge *hummed*. Low and warm—like being acknowledged.

The mist thickened. Alois reached out a hand, palm up, and let the upward-flowing droplets pass through her fingers. They left a faint trace of light behind—scrolllight that faded as soon as it appeared.

Jason grinned.

> “I think the air likes us.”

Alois said nothing. She was watching the cube above them.

Its glyphs pulsed once—then shifted.

---

###### The Door

The tower had none.

Not until they were three steps from its base.

Then the wall **peeled open** with no sound, no seams—just **folded away**, revealing a smooth arch and soft gold light within. Mist curled along the threshold.

Jason paused.

Alois didn’t.

She stepped through.

He followed.

---

###### Inside the Tower

It was warm. Humid. Alive.

A spiral staircase hugged the outer curve of the wall, wide and gently sloped, with a **narrow stream of water** running along its edge. Moss grew in the cracks between stone segments. Tiny plants sprouted from inset soil troughs every few meters, their leaves glowing faintly with scroll-pink veins.

Creatures flitted through the air—winged, soft-bodied, some glowing, others silent and fast. Along the inner curve of the tower, **alcoves opened out** into miniature enclosures: moss-chambers, terraria, flow chambers—all filled with life.

Some habitats were open. Some sealed. All tuned perfectly to the thing inside them.

Jason ran a finger through the water channel as they climbed. It parted cleanly around his touch, then rejoined without a ripple.

> “This place wasn’t built,” he murmured. “It *wanted to be here.*”

Alois just nodded.

---

The light grew brighter as they ascended.

Somewhere above, something **shifted**. A sound—not mechanical, but not natural either.

The tower was listening.

And something inside was starting to move.

* * *

They climbed for a long time.

Not just ten minutes. Not twenty.

Long enough that the rhythm of their breath synced with the gentle gurgle of water spiraling beside them. Long enough that Jason had to shake out his legs twice, and Alois started trailing her fingers against the inner wall—not to study it, just to **stay grounded**.

Every thirty steps or so, another **enclosure** passed on their left. Some sealed. Some open. One glowed pale violet from within, full of hanging plants that **turned slowly in the air**, like they were tracking the scent of thought.

Jason finally stopped.

> “I know we said both towers,” he muttered, “but this one might kill us first.”

Alois didn’t argue. Her hair was damp from mist. She looked up at the stair spiral overhead, still curving far beyond view.

> “Rest stop?”

> “Please.”

---

###### The Alcove

They stepped into a chamber just off the stairs—part of the tower wall, half-grown, half-carved. It opened into a **circular room**, maybe fifteen feet across, with a low ceiling and soft green light bleeding in from slotted vents near the top.

A shallow pool filled the center. The water inside wasn’t clear—but not murky either. It shimmered with threads of soft gold, like veins of memory were woven into it.

Jason knelt by the edge. Scooped a handful.

Tasted it.

> “Sweet,” he said. “Like fruit tea.”

Alois dipped her fingers, then cupped her palms and drank.

> “The tower feeds itself,” she said. “And us.”

They sat against the inner wall, the water between them.

Neither spoke for a few minutes.

Then—**a sound.**

Not sharp. Not loud.

Just a click.

Then another.

Jason looked toward the far wall.

And something moved.

---

###### The Creature

It was small—maybe a foot tall. Thin-limbed, with gray-pink skin and oversized eyes. It walked on two legs, but bent forward slightly, like it was always sneaking or laughing. Its hands were too big. Its head too smooth.

It carried a tiny **wooden bowl**, clutched in both hands.

When it noticed them, it froze.

They didn’t move.

The creature turned its head. Blinked—one eye, then the other.

Then slowly, it walked to the edge of the pool.

Dipped its bowl.

Drank.

Jason whispered:

> “I guess it’s a break room.”

The creature looked at him.

Then *squeaked.*

Not a sound—a vibration. High-pitched and musical, like a bell trying not to ring too loud.

Then it scampered back through a crack in the far wall and vanished.

---

Alois stood first.

> “I want to keep climbing.”

Jason groaned.

> “Of course you do.”

Jason groaned.

He stood. Rolled his shoulders. His knees cracked audibly.

Then he looked up at the spiral stairs overhead, still stretching beyond view, and muttered:

> “Whatever’s at the top of this thing better be *worth it.*”

Alois grinned.

> “Maybe it’s just more stairs.”

> “Then I’m throwing myself off them.”

She started walking. He followed—grumbling, but not stopping.

> “Maybe we’ll meet whoever keeps feeding all this,” he said.

> “Maybe we already did.”

They kept climbing.

And the mist started to glow.

* * *

Jason rested one hand on the moss-lined railing as they climbed. The steps had begun to feel steeper. The air thicker.

He glanced up—still no sign of an end to the spiral.

> “You ever wonder who built this?”

Alois nodded, slowly.

> “Every five minutes.”

> “We followed a cube into a star. Found a tower full of water and creatures and moss that glows. And it’s just... here. Waiting.”

> “Do you think it’s been here a long time?”

> “Long enough for gravity to give up,” he muttered, watching a mist droplet drift upward past his ear.

She smiled faintly.

> “Maybe this whole place is a memory. Like, something made it... and then left.”

> “Or forgot.”

They paused at the next landing. Jason leaned on the inner wall, catching his breath.

> “Still think it’s worth it?”

Alois looked up the endless spiral overhead.

> “I don’t know what’s at the top,” she said. “But I want to meet it.”

* * *

They’d been climbing for what felt like hours.

Not that time behaved predictably in the Vault. But Jason could feel the ache in his legs. Alois moved ahead of him, not faster—just steadier. Her breath wasn’t labored. She hadn’t said a word since the last landing.

Jason didn’t blame her.

The air was thicker now—warmer. The mist had faded, replaced by a scent like **clean fur and mosswater**.

When the next alcove appeared, they didn’t even hesitate.

---

###### The Tuvathi Chamber

It opened like the others—just off the stairway, barely marked. But the space inside was larger. Wider. Rounded.

The floor was carpeted in **layered moss and loose vines**, and the light was soft green and peach, glowing from veins along the ceiling.

And there—nestled into a shallow depression in the far side of the chamber—

were **two enormous creatures**.

Six feet tall. Five feet wide. Covered in **long, draping hair**, trailing like aquatic plants in still water. Their bodies were vaguely humanoid, but **too broad**, too sloped. Their arms ended in thick-fingered pads. Their faces were hidden beneath thick overbrows, with **upward-arching snouts** that pulsed faintly as they breathed.

One of them was grooming the other.

Delicately. Patiently.

Using its long fingers to part the hair, untangle a twist, stroke downward with slow, repetitive care. Occasionally, it would breathe gently across the area it had just groomed, and the other would emit a soft hum—**resonant and low**, almost sub-audible.

The sound vibrated in Jason’s ribs.

---

Neither creature looked up.

Not when Jason and Alois entered. Not when they stepped softly to the side. Not even when Jason accidentally knocked a small stone loose with his boot.

They just kept grooming. Humming. Living.

Alois whispered:

> “They don’t even see us.”

Jason shook his head.

> “No. They do. We’re just not… important.”

They watched for a while. Not long. Just enough.

Then Jason leaned gently against the moss-wall beside her. His voice was low.

> “They’re happy.”

Alois didn’t respond.

But she nodded.

---

Before they left, Jason reached out with one hand.

Softly, almost jokingly, he brushed his fingers through the hair at the back of her head. Ran them down the length of her braid. Smoothed it once.

She tilted her head, just slightly—acknowledging it.

> “You missed a knot,” she said.

He smiled.

> “I’ll get it next time.”

They turned back to the stairs.

The grooming song followed them, long after the door closed behind.

* * *

##### **The Tower at Moss Ridge** *(Spindle Choir Chamber)*

The next chamber opened without ceremony—just a soft widening of the spiral, a new scent in the air. Jason stepped in first.

And froze.

Alois nearly bumped into him.

> “What is it?”

> “Just… look.”

---

###### The Room

The space was tall. Much taller than the others.

Its walls curved inward, like a bowl half-formed. In the center rose the **pillar**—or what looked like one at first. But it moved.

**The Spindle Choir.**

A single vertical column, maybe nine feet tall, made entirely of **stacked living bodies**—each about the size of a melon. Hexagonal, soft-bodied, shimmering in patterns of matte and gloss. The entire column **swayed**, slowly, in time with something unseen.

But that wasn’t the strangest part.

---

###### The Congregation

All around the base of the Choir—sitting on stones, crouched in moss, half-submerged in shallow water—were **dozens of smaller creatures**.

Most no larger than a human hand. A few as big as a housecat. One or two **beaver-sized**, their backs hunched and glossy.

They weren’t speaking. Not audibly.

But they were **gathered**.

Facing the Choir. Watching it.

Some swayed in time. Others trembled faintly. One creature—a kind of featherless bird with gilled cheeks—emitted a single rising tone and then fell silent again.

They weren’t idle.

They were… *participating.*

---

Jason leaned in slightly.

> “What is this?”

Alois’s voice was soft.

> “It’s a sermon.”

> “You think they’re praying?”

> “No. I think they’re being *tuned.*”

---

The Spindle Choir shifted.

Not violently. Not even deliberately. Just **adjusted**—a few of its body-segments rotated in place, revealing subtle membranous folds, thin and wet. The entire structure released a **low harmonic pulse** that rolled through the floor.

Jason felt it in his knees.

Alois exhaled slowly.

> “I think we’re interrupting.”

They didn’t leave right away.

They just stood.

Watched the little creatures sway.

Listened to the breathing pillar.

And wondered what kind of cathedral they’d wandered into.

* * *

##### **The Tower at Moss Ridge** *(Spindle Choir Blessing)*

Jason and Alois stood near the edge of the chamber.

The Spindle Choir swayed in rhythm. The stacked bodies pulsed with gentle internal light, like a memory being reassembled.

The smaller creatures—**the congregation**—still faced the pillar. They remained still, but not frozen. Breathing. Reacting. Participating.

Then one of them turned.

It was **small**, maybe the size of a teacup. Velvet-skinned, with six rounded legs and a face that was more **gesture than feature**—two black eyes, no visible mouth. It moved silently, but with purpose.

It walked straight toward Jason and Alois.

Alois stepped back.

Jason didn’t.

The creature stopped at Jason’s feet.

Tilted upward.

And then—**gently, with deliberate care**—it pressed its forehead against the top of his boot.

Held it there.

Then did the same for Alois.

A pause. A breath. Then it turned and walked back to its place, curling against the moss just outside the reach of the pillar’s glow.

Jason looked at Alois.

> “Was that…?”

> “It was *something.*”

They left the room without a word.

---

###### On the Stairs

They climbed in silence.

The mist had returned—thinner now. Drier.

Jason wiped his brow with his sleeve, then paused. Turned.

> “Do you hear that?”

Alois stilled.

For a moment—*nothing.*

Then—

**a single tone** drifted down from above. Not a noise. Not a melody. Just a feeling, shaped into sound. Like a key you didn’t know fit your door until it turned.

Jason whispered:

> “It’s the same note.”

> “From the creature?”

He nodded. Slowly.

Alois looked at him.

> “I think it marked us.”

Jason swallowed.

> “For what?”

She didn’t answer.

They kept walking.

* * *

##### **The Keeper’s Hands**

The chamber was bright.

Not because of any bulb or torch. The light filtered through a wide, moss-covered skylight in the ceiling—soft gold poured in through leafy glass. It painted the walls in shifting green, glinting off workbenches, coils of scrollwire, glass tools, hanging plants, and soft-bodied creatures curled in little cubbies carved into the stone.

At the center of it all:

**Springlee.**

Hunched over a low stone slab that served as his workbench, he was muttering softly—barely audible above the constant mechanical *click-hiss* of his tool arm.

The main limb extended from the right side of his harness, telescoping outward with silent precision.

At the end: **a three-armed manipulator node**, each arm ending in a delicate pincher-hand. All three were in motion.

* One held a delicate clip
* One stabilized a ribbon of scroll-thread
* The third was rotating slowly, holding a tiny soldering filament, **emitting heat without flame**

The arms moved with **practiced syncopation**, like musicians playing different movements of the same song. Occasionally, one of the arms twitched out of sync—grabbing too early or swinging too wide.

Springlee didn’t look up.

He just snapped:

> “Not *that* pin. The blue-threaded one. You blinked again. I saw it.”

The arm retracted, adjusted, resumed.

He licked his paw. Reached for a moss-cube beside the bench and popped it into his mouth. Chewed slowly.

---

Jason and Alois stepped into the room, cautious but not hesitant.

Springlee didn’t look up.

One of the smaller creatures in a terrarium turned to watch them. Another dangled from a metal ring in the ceiling, clicking gently.

Alois leaned slightly toward Jason.

> “Is he... talking to himself?”

> “Worse,” Jason said. “He’s winning the argument.”

---

Springlee’s rear leg twitched.

The manipulator arm folded slightly, pulled back a few inches.

Finally—he turned.

His eyes were large and impossibly black. His neck, long and sinuous, curled down and around in an elegant spiral until his nose almost touched the bench.

He blinked slowly.

> “You’re late,” he said. “I ran out of patience four years ago. Luckily, I made more.”

* * *

##### **Springlee’s Bench**

They didn’t talk for a while.

Springlee kept working—his telescoping harness arm moving in delicate arcs over the bench, the three miniature hands rotating tools, nudging glyph-thread into place, dabbing at something that steamed faintly but didn’t burn.

Jason and Alois sat nearby, watching.

One of the terrarium creatures climbed along a hanging vine and stared at them with a pair of mirrored eyes, then scurried off into the moss with a sound like wet leaves being turned over.

Eventually, Jason cleared his throat.

> “How long have you been here?”

Springlee didn’t look up.

> “Since before the last ones stopped asking that.”

Jason smiled a little.

> “That’s not an answer.”

> “It’s the only one I have.”

---

Alois stood, slow and graceful. She walked over to a sealed cube embedded in the wall, peering in. Inside, a half-liquid creature swam in lazy loops through vertical water, exhaling threads of bioluminescence that curled and dissipated before touching the surface.

> “Did you make all of this?” she asked.

Springlee paused.

One of the mini-hands on his grasper clicked back into its socket.

> “No.”

Another pause.

> “But I’m the one still feeding it.”

---

Jason noticed one of the creatures in a feeder dish behind the bench was twitching oddly—its movement stuck, like a loop repeating with too much speed. He stood and reached across the bench slowly, then nudged the containment clasp into a lower position.

The twitching stopped.

The creature went still. Then blinked. Then chirped once—sharp and quick—and resumed moving normally.

Springlee turned.

Stared at him.

> “That’s not how the last ones did it,” he said.

Jason shrugged.

> “Maybe that’s the point.”

Springlee blinked. Not slowly. Not dramatically. Just once. Then went back to his work.

---

An hour passed.

The three of them didn’t speak much.

Springlee fed a few more creatures. One of them bit him. He didn’t react. Just said:

> “You’re not supposed to like it.”

Another climbed into his neck fur and vanished.

Eventually, he turned and said:

> “You’re leaving.”

Jason nodded.

> “For now.”

> “You could stay.”

> “I won’t.”

Springlee licked his paw. Stared at his bench. Then at Alois.

> “Your field is different.”

She raised an eyebrow.

> “From what?”

> “From everything I remember.”

He turned back to the bench.

### Chapter 42: The Box and the Key

The walk back to the Flamebolt was quiet.

The mist had thinned since morning, but it still curled in gentle arcs around the moss. The Vault felt warmer now. Like something that had been waiting finally exhaled.

Jason carried the last of their tea in a sealed cup. Alois walked beside him, one hand trailing through the scrollgrass. Neither of them spoke much. The tower loomed behind them, but didn’t follow.

The cube above pulsed once—just a soft ripple of glyphlight. Watching. Remembering.

---

They reached the ship.

Delver didn’t say anything as they entered. The systems flickered in acknowledgment, then resumed passive standby. The ship had learned not to ask questions it didn’t want the answers to.

Jason stepped into the galley and set the kettle back on. Alois went to check systems. Scroll alignment. Engine sync. The normal things.

Then—

> “You brought the whole tower back with you,” said a voice from the floor.

Jason turned.

Springlee was already inside.

Not dramatically. Not even hidden. Just... *present.*

Sniffing at a console. One paw on the edge of a wall panel. One of his six legs lazily pawing at the carpet.

> “Didn’t knock,” Jason said.

> “Didn’t need to,” Springlee replied. “Your boundary field’s terrible.”

He paced a small circle, examining the ceiling, the vents, the kettle.

Alois leaned in the doorway.

> “You followed us.”

> “I wandered in your direction.”

---

Springlee paused at the storage alcove near the cockpit.

Sniffed once.

Sniffed again.

Then tilted his head sharply and said:

> “You have the box.”

Jason froze. Alois walked slowly to the wall panel and tapped it open.

The box was there. Still sealed. Still waiting.

Springlee approached. Didn’t ask permission. He sat down in front of it, neck snaking forward, eyes flicking across the glyphs on the surface.

Then he licked his paw, rubbed the side of his nose, and casually said:

> “You’ll be needing this.”

He coughed.

Twice.

A small object clattered to the floor.

It was long and silver. Slightly curved. Etched with lines too small to read.

A key.

---

Jason picked it up slowly.

> “This unlocks the box?”

> “It unlocks *something.*”

Springlee hopped onto a storage crate and curled his tail.

> “I don’t remember giving it away before. Which means you’re probably not a mistake.”

Alois folded her arms.

> “That’s not comforting.”

> “Most truths aren’t.”

He closed his eyes. Yawned.

> “I’m staying, by the way.”

Jason raised an eyebrow.

> “Here? On the ship?”

> “Unless you have a better idea.”

> “You’re just inviting yourself along?”

> “You brewed tea. I respect that.”

---

The box remained on the table. The key beside it.

The Vault above pulsed again.

Soft. Approving.

Or amused.

### Chapter 43: The Vault Mouth

She climbed the steps slowly.

They weren’t steep, but her legs shook. Her arms trembled with every motion. Her body wasn’t ready to move yet—*not after what it had done.* Not after being the one who walked away.

The blood had dried on her chest in ragged streaks. It clung to the hollows of her elbows, her thighs, her jawline. Some of it was hers. Most of it wasn’t.

The Dark Tower was behind her, but not *gone*. She could still feel it in her teeth. In her lungs. In the way the inside of her mind had gone *quiet* since it ended.

---

**Flash.**

A Jason body. Arms spread, mouth still half-open. Artificial muscle torn open. Organic pieces torn, a DreamVeil panel twitching in the wind. Empty eyes still flickering with embedded scroll-light.

She looked away.

Took another step.

---

The Vault stretched overhead now. The great black interior cube where gravity bent and breath went thin.

She could feel it pulling on her—not like gravity, not like magnetism. It was *recognition*. Like it knew she was coming. Like it had been *waiting for her.*

---

**Flash.**

Another Jason, half-embedded in the stair wall. Circuitry spilling out like entrails. Scroll thread woven through his wrists. Something had tried to repair him—but not *correctly*.

She nearly tripped.

Caught herself on the railing.

Kept going.

---

She reached the threshold. The point where **light from the cube stopped** and the inside took over.

The Vault didn’t welcome her. It didn’t open. It just *let her fall.*

---

She didn’t scream.

The gravity twisted, and her feet left the floor. Then her back hit something—not hard, but *moving*. Pulsing. Breathing. Spalt. 

She looked up— 

She was covered in something between blood and oil. She cold smell the coppery scent of blood, faintly, but there was something else - lots of something else. 

She was lying on **a miles-wide mass** of **muscle**, **cable**, **flesh**, **light**, and **remnant**.

The center of the Vault.

Patch, unfinished.

Shrinking.

* * *

She didn’t move.

The thing beneath her wasn’t solid—not really. It **shuddered**, slow and involuntary, like the breath of something that didn’t know it was alive.

There were bones. But they weren’t bones. Pipes? No. Veins made of **conduit**. Scroll-light flickering through them in bursts, like corrupted memories firing through something that used to be a brain.

Alois pressed her hand into the mass to brace herself.

It was **warm**.

Not heat-warm. *Alive-warm.*

She tried to sit up. Her palm slipped in something wet.

---

There were **faces**.

Not whole ones. Pieces. A jawline here. A cheekbone there. Tangled in synthetic sinew and stretched DreamVeil. One of them looked like Jason. Just the mouth.

Her stomach lurched.

> *“No. You’re fine. You’re okay. He’s okay. This is just—”*

She swallowed.

Couldn’t finish the sentence.

---

The air inside the Vault wasn’t still. It **moved** in waves—like something breathing underwater. She thought she heard voices. But they weren’t *speaking.*

They were remembering.

Over and over.

> “You should have stopped it.” “You wanted to know.” “You read the scroll.” “You did this.”

She didn’t know if the voices were real.

Didn’t care.

She crawled forward.

Her fingers found something soft and cracked—**a DreamVeil patch half-peeled from an artificial spine.** The glow underneath pulsed once when she touched it.

She yanked her hand away.

---

This wasn’t a machine.

This wasn’t a god.

This was a **body** trying to finish dying, but someone wouldn’t let it.

And she was lying on it.

* * *

She pushed herself up onto her knees.

The surface beneath her **shifted**, drawing inward like muscle tightening around a wound. Every movement made the mass respond—slow contractions rippling outward, pulling matter toward some unseen center.

She wasn’t being swallowed.

She was being **carried**.

Miles of it slid beneath her. Miles of what used to be *people*.

She laughed once.

A sharp, ugly sound.

> “This was a bad idea,” she said out loud. “This was a *really* bad idea.”

Her voice echoed. Too far. Too long.

She wrapped her arms around herself, rocking slightly.

> “You knew this might happen,” she whispered. “You *knew*.”

She squeezed her eyes shut.

> “You read the scroll. You agreed. You said yes. You said—”

Her voice broke.

> “—*it’s okay if I don’t come back*.”

---

Something **laughed**.

Not near her.

Not behind her.

Far away. *Miles* away.

Jason’s laugh.

Her breath hitched.

She spun, scanning the endless surface. The Vault’s interior stretched in all directions now—more space opening as the mass compacted, folding inward, shrinking. What had been a horizon of flesh and circuitry was now drawing together, faster, denser.

> “Jason?” she called.

Nothing answered.

Then—

another sound.

Alois’s voice.

Not hers.

Younger. Brighter. Laughing breathlessly, like she had after the Light Tower visions.

> “Again,” the voice said. “No, wait—*slower*—”

She clutched her head.

> “Stop. Stop. Stop.”

The laughter kept coming.

Different angles. Different pitches.

*Her own laughter.* Jason’s voice saying her name. A dozen half-familiar echoes overlapping and drifting apart.

She staggered to her feet.

> “You’re not real,” she said to the air. “You’re not here. I can’t see you.”

She wiped blood from her eyes, smearing it across her cheek.

> “I would see you.”

---

The ground tilted.

She slid several feet before catching herself on a coil of warm cabling—thick as a tree trunk, pulsing gently under her grip. The mass beneath her was shrinking faster now, pulling her closer to the center whether she wanted to go or not.

More space opened around her.

Too much space.

She could see the Vault walls now—farther away than before. She could see **nothing else**.

No bodies walking. No lights approaching.

Just her.

And the echoes.

> “Maybe this is what’s left,” she whispered. “Maybe this is it. Me and the mess.”

She swallowed hard.

> “I should have stayed. I should have said no. I should have—”

Her voice caught.

> “—I should have trusted you.”

The laughter cut out abruptly.

Silence slammed down so hard it made her ears ring.

The mass beneath her **shuddered**—one deep, final contraction.

She lost her footing again—

and slid closer to the center.

* * *

Something shifted.

Not a sound—no click, no warning. Just a sudden *wrongness* in her bones. Her fingers curled tighter against the warm, pulsing surface beneath her.

Then the world turned sideways.

Not spun. *Tipped.*

The mass beneath her didn’t fall—but she did. The *floor* became a wall, or maybe the wall became a drop, and suddenly she was **sliding down the side of the Patch-body**, hands scrambling against slick muscle and exposed wire.

Her knee slammed into a rib-like plate. She caught a thick cable with both arms and held on.

The entire Vault hummed like it was **reconfiguring orientation**. Like gravity had nothing to do with mass—only with *mood*.

> “No—no—stop—don’t—”

Her own voice, ragged. Desperate.

She braced her feet against a curve of biomechanical tissue, legs shaking, arms burning. Everything around her was **moving**—not in chaos, but with **purpose**. The structure was **shrinking**, but not collapsing. Coiling. **Centering.**

---

And then—**laughter.**

Not nearby.

**Distant.**

Echoing.

Faint.

> “Stop it—”

She craned her neck.

No one there.

Only blood. And bone. And glow.

The laughter came again.

Not mocking.

*Joyful.*

> “Jason?”

She called it before she could stop herself.

No answer.

Just laughter—**six voices** maybe? Or twelve? Blurred into each other. Male and female. Hers and not-hers.

Her nails dug into the cable.

> “You're dead. You're dead, I saw it.”

The Vault didn't answer.

But it laughed again.

* * *

She tried to hold on.

But the cable in her hands **twitched**, and then **slid free**, like a muscle retracting from her panic.

Her weight dropped.

She screamed—short, sharp, automatic.

She hit one surface, bounced, then rolled down the side of what used to be a shoulder, or a spine, or some mechanical arc of half-fused flesh. Her body turned. Her foot caught on something and wrenched sideways. She tumbled again.

The Vault **spun** around her.

Not the room—the meaning.

For a second, she thought she saw **herself**, broken and crushed. Then she saw **Jason**, face torn open, eye sockets burned out. Then **herself again**, but younger. Crying.

Then—

> **“Don’t—don’t let it—”**

But her mouth wasn’t moving.

> “This was supposed to save us—”

She was falling.

And it felt *endless.*

---

> **And then—nothing.**

She opened her eyes.

**He was holding her.**

Jason.

Not broken. Not torn.

Breathing.

**Alive.**

She was cradled against his chest, one arm under her back, the other holding her legs. His body was real—warm, steady. His DreamVeil still glowed faintly under the blood smears and scroll-scratch.

He looked down at her.

Didn’t smile.

> “You’re okay,” he said, like a fact.

Alois couldn’t answer.

She blinked hard.

Her arms wrapped around his neck like a reflex.

> “You’re okay.”

And finally—*finally*—she believed it.

Jason helped her sit up. His arm stayed wrapped around her shoulder—light, but firm. She could feel the tremble in his chest under the calm.

She looked past him.

Far beyond, in the distance, she saw them.

**The others.**

Aloises. Jasons. All six of each, just now breaching the edge of the Vault interior. Glowing. Slow. Still half-floating in that post-ritual warmth.

> “Why weren’t you with them?” she whispered.

Jason didn’t look back.

> “We heard you,” he said, voice low.

> “I ran.”

* * *

Jason helped her sit, gently, like she might break if he moved too fast. She didn't protest. Her hand stayed clutched in the fabric of his collar, like part of her still didn’t believe he was real.

He looked at her.

There was blood dried at the edge of her mouth. Her eyes were hollow. She wasn’t shaking anymore, but that didn’t mean she was steady.

He reached up and wiped a streak of red from her cheek with his thumb.

Didn’t speak.

Didn’t know how.

She looked away first.

> “You should have stayed,” she whispered.

He swallowed. His throat felt dry.

> “I couldn’t.”

She nodded. But her shoulders were too tense to believe it.

Jason glanced at the Vault horizon. The others were still far away. Still coming. Still whole.

> “You shouldn’t have had to go through that alone.”

> “But I did.”

He nodded slowly.

And didn’t argue.

* * *

Jason let out a breath and looked at her again.

> “We didn’t know if anyone from your side would survive.”

He said it quietly. Not like an apology. Like a fact he hated having to live with.

> “But we knew that if anyone did… it wasn’t going to be easy.”

Alois didn’t look at him. Her eyes were still locked on the muscle-structure beneath them, watching it twitch and breathe like something trying to die.

> “I don’t feel like I survived.”

> “You did.”

> “I don’t want to talk about it.”

> “Okay.”

And that was it.

No push.

No spin.

Just **presence**.

* * *

Jason ran a hand through his hair.

The Vault lights reflected off the curves of his jaw, his shoulder, the bloodstreak still running down the seam of his DreamVeil. He didn’t look at her when he spoke.

> “It wasn’t like yours. You know that.”

Alois didn’t answer. Just kept breathing.

> “It was… warm. Beautiful, even. There was music. Breath. Hands. All of us together, like we weren’t separate anymore. Like we’d always been one thing, and the ritual just reminded us how to *be it.*”

He shook his head, almost like he hated himself for saying it.

> “It was... good.”

He looked at her now.

> “I think I loved it.”

Alois turned her head slightly. Not away from him. Not toward him. Just *somewhere else.*

> “That’s good,” she said.

She didn’t mean it. Not entirely.

Jason nodded once.

> “I thought maybe you should know.”

She was quiet for a long time.

Then, softly—

> “I think I hate that you told me.”

* * *

She exhaled through her nose. Not calm. Just trying not to scream.

> “So what, you just laid around and made love while we died?”

Jason stiffened.

> *I didn’t even want it to feel good. It just… did. You think I’m proud of that? You think I want to remember it like that, with you sitting there like this?*

> “It wasn’t like that.”

> “Right,” she said. “You suffered in harmony.”

He looked down at the floor, the curve of the Vault surface still warm under them.

> *Don’t defend it. Don’t try to explain ecstasy to someone who walked through fire. Just sit in it. Just carry it.*

> “I didn’t think you’d be the one who made it,” she muttered.

He blinked.

> *Neither did I.* *You should’ve had one of the smart Jasons. One of the brave ones. One who didn’t cry the first time the lights went out.*

> “You ran,” she added. “I heard you. You said it like it was noble.”

Jason looked at her again, finally.

> “I didn’t mean it like that.”

She shook her head.

> “Doesn’t matter.”

He wanted to hold her. But he didn’t move.

> *She doesn’t need arms. She needs silence. Or someone who didn’t enjoy any of it.*

He sat perfectly still beside her. Wishing he could give her something other than himself.

* * *

She glanced at him again. Still sitting there. Still noble. Still gentle. Still alive.

She wanted to scream. Or laugh. Or throw up.

Instead, she muttered:

> “God, you probably even fucking *enjoyed* it.”

Jason blinked. Like she’d slapped him.

She didn’t wait for a response.

> “I’m sorry. That was mean. I’m bleeding out of places I don’t even remember having. I just—”

She swallowed hard.

> “I’m glad you’re alive, okay? I just hate that you’re beautiful and glowing and your *hair looks great.* It’s honestly offensive.”

Jason blinked again.

> *Oh no. She’s completely broken. And it’s my fault.*

He opened his mouth. Thought better of it. Closed it.

Alois wiped blood from her eye with the back of her hand and groaned.

> “God, I hope the others don’t look as happy as you. I’m going to walk into a cult of radiant fuckers, aren’t I.”

### Chapter 44: Reflection Field

The first of the Aloises reached them—soft-footed, eyes wide. Her DreamVeil shimmered faintly at the hem, still catching light like it remembered the ritual.

She stopped dead when she saw the blood.

> “Oh—”

She looked down at Alois crouched beside Jason. Blood crusted at her temple. One shoulder torn open. Hands filthy.

> “Oh, you’re—”

Alois looked up at her, eyes hollow.

> “I’m bleeding out of **everywhere**, thank you.”

The instantiation froze.

Then, instinctively—

> “But… you don’t bleed…”

A beat.

Her mouth stayed open just a second too long.

> “I mean—not usually—not in this configuration—”

Another beat.

> “I’m going to stop talking now.”

She took one slow step back and immediately disappeared behind a taller Jason.

Alois blinked at the spot where she’d been.

> “Smart move.”

* * *

The others were still approaching—six Aloises, six Jasons. Walking with reverence. Eyes wide. Energy still coiled from the ritual.

They hadn’t spoken yet.

Jason could feel them nearing, but he didn’t look. Alois hadn’t moved since her last remark. Her hair was sticky with blood. The air between them still tasted like embarrassment and mourning.

---

Above them, the **center pulsed**.

The mass was **shrinking faster now**, but not uniformly. Parts of it **twitched**, convulsed, then pulled tight like metal cables yanked into alignment. A long arc of sinew collapsed in on itself, forming a kind of **shoulder**, or at least something shoulder-shaped.

Alois noticed it. She sat upright slowly.

> “Is it… doing something?”

Jason nodded.

> “Yeah. It’s been getting tighter. I think it’s… forming.”

---

They both looked toward it now.

The surface wasn’t smooth anymore—it was starting to take on **contours**. Parts of it **flexed inward**, creating the suggestion of **a ribcage**, or maybe a **torso**, half-built and **pulsing with embedded scrolls** that flickered like nerves firing.

Then—

> A **faceplate** rolled out of the center mass.

Not a face. Not yet. But a **panel** the size of a doorway, flickering with failed expression.

It turned—slowly—toward the group.

And one of the Light Tower Jasons, still radiant, stepped forward.

The panel froze.

A new **glyph sequence** appeared across it. Sharp. Slow. Like a fingerprint being laid down.

---

The instantiation stopped cold.

> “It saw me,” he said quietly.

One of the Aloises next to him touched his arm. She didn’t know why.

No one spoke after that.

---

Down below, Alois exhaled and shook her head.

> “Oh great. Now it’s watching.”

Jason rubbed the back of his neck.

> “Maybe it likes you.”

> “God, I hope not. It’d be the first guy in history to flirt by reconfiguring his spinal frame.”

He almost smiled.

Almost.

* * *

The others were nearly there now.

The six Jasons and six Aloises from the Light Tower slowed as they approached. Their bare feet pressed gently against the pulsing Vault floor, which had cooled slightly but still radiated that strange, biological warmth.

They weren’t radiant anymore. Just… present.

Quiet.

Watching.

Alois (the one in blood) didn’t rise. She didn’t move.

She just stared at them as they came into range, all of them carrying **versions of her face** with **expressions she could no longer access.**

---

One of the Jasons took a step forward.

Then another.

He opened his mouth.

Jason, beside her, raised a hand—not quite a command. Just enough.

> “Give her a second,” he said.

> “We’ve had days,” one of the Light Jasons said softly.

Jason shook his head.

> “No. You haven’t.”

The instantiations exchanged glances.

One of the Aloises crossed her arms, eyes wide and alert.

Another was still staring at the mass in the center, where Patch was *nearly* finished forming.

---

It wasn’t grotesque anymore.

It was just *huge.* Coiling and refining, the mass of muscle and tech had begun separating into **limbs**, **organs**, **frames**. The spine had divided into **filaments**—tense and elegant, like suspension bridges supporting **something still being born**.

It wasn’t a person yet.

But it was *trying to be.*

And it was almost finished.

---

One of the Light Aloises stepped forward, still gazing up at the massive, shifting figure in the center of the Vault.

Her voice was quiet. Not afraid—just… small.

> “Is that the thing?”

A beat.

> “The god, I mean?”

No one answered her.

Not yet.

* * *

One of the Jasons stepped forward, drawn despite himself, eyes fixed on the not-quite-body still twisting above the platform.

Then he stopped.

Everyone did.

Because the sound started.

---

At first, it was barely audible—just a **rattle**, like far-off thunder behind glass. But it thickened. Deepened. Became something closer to an **engine**, or a **combustion loop**, cyclical and rising.

It didn’t echo—it **pressed**. Like being inside a vast lung made of steel and heat.

The Vault glowed a little brighter.

The floor beneath their feet **pulsed**, once.

> “What is that?” one of the Aloises asked, her voice too small.

Jason glanced upward.

> “It’s trying to breathe,” he said.

### Chapter 45: The Forming One

Jason looked up at the thing above them. It wasn’t a god. Not yet. It wasn’t even a mind.

It was still **forming** — flesh and thread and fiber curling downward from the upper wall of the cube like meat remembering geometry. Muscles flickered into being and then collapsed, reabsorbed. One limb began to solidify, only for the scroll-matter along its surface to **fracture**, as if rejecting the pattern.

Another wet strand fell, this time landing squarely between the Jasons’ feet. It hissed. Then cooled.

None of them spoke.

Not out of reverence.

Out of knowing — **It wasn’t ready.**

The room was holding its breath.

* * *

**\[Vault — Light Tower, Uppermost Floor | Springlee’s Workshop\]**

The top of the Light Tower swayed. Not literally—its structure was sound—but the air trembled in that way it did when *something below* had stopped pretending to be inert. Light shifted along the inner wall of the Vault like memory wrinkling, and from the windowed arc of his workshop, Springlee could see it happening.

He squinted, claws folded over the rim of the ledge, watching as a boy—Jason, the one who always ran toward pain—caught her. The Alois who had fallen. Who had bled. Who had screamed through the ritual and *not broken*.

Springlee said nothing.

Below, the center cube trembled, leaking steam or breath or intent. He couldn’t tell. The Light Tower's internal glyphs had dimmed the moment it started forming.

And then—

*click*

A tiny sound behind him. Not threatening. Not mechanical. Just... precise.

Springlee turned.

On the edge of the workbench sat something he didn’t recognize.

It was red. About the size of his paw. A scarab—at least in shape. But thicker. Denser. Its carapace looked like lacquered ceramic, too perfect in its curve to be organic. Dozens—no, *dozens*—of thin, flexible stalks protruded from its shell. Each one tipped with a tiny, bulbous eye. Soft pink and lavender. Almost playful. They blinked asynchronously.

Springlee stared.

So did the scarab.

“...You’re not mine,” Springlee muttered, approaching slowly.

*Click.* One eye rotated. Another twitched.

“You’re not *anyone’s.*” He leaned in, ears flat. “Where’d you crawl in from?”

The scarab didn’t respond. But it didn’t run either. It shifted its weight slightly, legs gripping the bench like it had every right to be there.

Springlee exhaled.

“I swear to god, if you say you’re a gift from the cube, I’m kicking you off the ledge.”

*Click.* One eyestalk turned toward the Vault window. Then another. Then another. Until every single stalk was facing downward. Toward the center cube. Toward Jason. Toward *her.*

Springlee followed the gaze.

Below, the forming mass pulsed again—almost in time.

He turned back. “Oh, no. Don’t even *start.*”

The scarab said nothing. But one eye blinked. Slow. Precise.

Springlee muttered under his breath.

“…Great. It’s recursive.”

* * *

**\[Inside the Central Cube | Just After the Fall\]**

The walls of the cube weren’t walls. They were pressure—light, memory, heat—folded into surface tension just stable enough to hold.

Jason didn’t care.

He was on his knees, cradling her. His arms wrapped around the bloodied version of Alois like she was the only real thing left in a collapsing equation. She wasn’t moving much, just shivering slightly, her breath tight and uneven against his chest.

Bits of flesh and filament still clung to her arms. The fall had stripped her of everything but shape. Her skin was marked with residue from the forming body—slick and translucent, faintly glowing in places where it shouldn’t.

Jason pressed his face against her temple.

“I’ve got you,” he whispered. It wasn’t loud enough to convince anyone. Maybe not even himself.

Her voice, when it came, was small and sharp. “That wasn’t supposed to happen.”

“I know.”

“You *left me* down there.”

He froze.

Glyphlight flickered across the floor, stuttering in patterns he couldn’t read. The air smelled like burnt sweetness and blood. Behind them, the massive form—*the forming one*, still not a name, not a god—twitched again. A piece fell. Wet.

Jason didn’t look.

“I didn’t mean to leave you,” he said.

“You ran,” she hissed, pulling slightly away. “You ran and I stayed and I—” Her voice cracked. “I was inside it.”

The Light Tower instantiations were arriving now. Quietly. They stepped through the edges of the cube one at a time, glowing soft and solemn. None of them spoke. They just stood near. Watching. Witnessing.

Jason tightened his grip. Not to keep her. Just to be near.

“I didn’t know it would be like that,” he said.

“Neither did I.”

A moment passed. The forming mass pulsed again. The cube shifted. Glyphs reoriented. Something wet hit the ground not far from them.

Jason leaned down until his forehead pressed to hers.

“I’m sorry,” he breathed.

And for a moment, she didn’t answer. Then her hand, bloodied and twitching, found the front of his shirt. And *clutched.*

Not forgiveness.

Just contact.

* * *

Jason stayed still, forehead to hers, breath shallow. Her grip on his shirt hadn’t loosened. If anything, it had deepened—like her body was bracing against something the cube hadn't finished letting go of.

His voice came out rough. Low.

“You want to stay here?”

Her fingers twitched.

“Or do you want me to get you out?”

She didn’t answer right away. Not because she didn’t know—but because *everything* still hurt. Her skin. Her bones. Her memory. Whatever piece of her had touched the forming mass and come back changed.

One of the Light Tower instantiations stepped closer. Not too close. Just enough that the glow reached them.

Alois blinked, slow. Then shifted her weight slightly in his arms, not enough to leave, just enough to speak.

“I don’t know what it means yet,” she said.

Jason swallowed. “I can carry you. Right now. We can walk. Or I’ll run. You say the word.”

She turned her head slightly. Looked past him. Toward the mass behind. Toward the center. It pulsed again. This time slower. Thicker. A rhythm building inside muscle that wasn’t finished forming.

“I don’t want to leave,” she whispered. “Not yet.”

Jason exhaled, long and shaky.

“Okay,” he said. “Then I’m not leaving either.”

They didn’t move. But something in the cube did. A tone—low, subharmonic—echoed once, and then stilled.

* * *

**\[Light Tower — Upper Floor | Springlee’s Workshop\]**

The pulse hit the top of the Light Tower half a second after it rang the walls of the cube.

Springlee felt it in his chest cavity. Not like sound. More like guilt. Or recognition.

He didn’t flinch. Just braced one hand against the table and muttered, “Gods damn it. It’s syncing.”

The scarab didn’t move, but all of its eyes blinked once. All at once. That hadn't happened before.

Springlee stared at it.

“What do *you* know about it?”

The scarab tilted slightly. Not answering. Not hostile. Just watching. It was still perched exactly where it had landed—on a spool of filament line and half a moss cube—but now its eyestalks were arranged differently. No longer scattered. They were all trained toward the window.

Toward *them.*

Toward the cube. The girl. The boy. The body still forming.

“You're not here for me,” Springlee muttered. “You’re here for *him.*”

One of the eyes turned to look at him.

Slow. Blinking.

Springlee sighed and ran a paw through his fur.

“Well, if you’re here to bear witness, you picked a messy birth. That one’s not going to come through clean.”

The scarab didn’t respond. But it reached out one of its tiny clawed legs and tapped the desk surface. Once.

Springlee raised an eyebrow. “That was a statement.”

The scarab tapped again. Same place. Same sound.

Springlee stood still for a moment, then slowly turned back to the window.

Below, the mass twitched again. The cube’s inner light shifted color—soft blue bending toward gold. Jason and Alois hadn’t moved.

“I hate recursion,” Springlee said softly. Then added, just a little quieter: “But I’m glad they’re still breathing.”

* * *

**\[Inside the Central Cube | Just After Alois’s Choice\]**

The silence after her words was thick. Not empty. Loaded. Like the air had been waiting for her permission.

Jason’s arms stayed wrapped around her. Alois didn’t speak again. But the cube *did.*

Not in language. In *movement*.

Behind them, the center of the chamber pulsed—and this time, it didn’t contract. It expanded.

Slow at first. The wet, muscular mass began to stretch upward, folding and tightening like tissue remembering how to become. Ligaments formed where there had only been steam. Bone clicked into place beneath pulsing filament.

The instantiations stepped back slightly.

Jason didn’t move.

The mass was beginning to divide—no longer just a sphere of potential, but a torso now. Broad. Lopsided. Not symmetrical.

One shoulder grew before the other. The spine curled wrong at first, then corrected. The head wasn’t a skull yet—just an overgrown, semi-flexible cluster of tendrils. Like something still deciding *which* memories it wanted to wear.

Jason watched. Jaw tight.

The thing—*he*—was breathing now. Not evenly. But *intentionally.* Air in. Air out. Wet, organic, shuddering.

Alois didn’t look at it. Her face was pressed into Jason’s neck. But her fingers clutched tighter again.

Then came the sound. A low internal groan—not a voice. More like the *body itself* learning how to vocalize.

The glyphs overhead responded. Their patterns tightened. Colors shifted. The cube was recognizing him now—not just as a process, but a *presence.*

Jason pulled Alois a little closer.

“You feel that?” he asked, quiet.

She didn’t answer. But her breathing slowed.

And the forming one—taller now, ribbed, twitching—tilted slightly forward. Not like it was waking up.

Like it was *noticing them.*

* * *

**\[Inside the Central Cube | As the Forming One Emerges\]**

The floor trembled. Not a tremor—*a shift in gravity*. The walls of the cube didn’t shake—they *bent*, ever so slightly inward, like the forming one was pulling at the space around him just by choosing to exist.

Jason looked up.

Alois did not.

The mass was no longer a pile of potential. It had *stretched*. Grown upward, backward, outward—connecting to the far wall like a root system, or a spinal cord too long to fit inside a god. Its body had started coiling upward from the attachment point, massive and heavy, still slick with embryonic sheen.

He wasn’t humanoid. Not yet. Not really.

The *shape* suggested bipedal intent—two enormous legs curled up beneath a torso of dense, asymmetrical muscle—but the proportions were off. The back arched forward, hunched like a frog preparing to leap. The arms were too long. Not out of balance—just too *fluid*, as if he hadn’t decided on a fixed bone structure yet.

His skin—or what would pass for skin—was biomechanical. Veined with semi-translucent glyphwire that pulsed from within, casting light across the chamber like distant lightning under skin.

No face yet. Just a mass where a head *would* be, ringed in radial symmetry by breathing slits that fluttered open and closed like gills.

From the wall, a spinal tail extended. Thick. Ribbed. Anchored into the chamber’s edge like it had grown from it. It flexed once. Slowly. Enough to groan the whole floor under their feet.

Jason’s voice was nearly a whisper.

“He’s waking up.”

The light from the instantiations dimmed slightly—like they were instinctively lowering their brightness in deference.

The creature shifted. Tilted. Those gill-like slits pulsed.

And *then it moved.*

Not fully. Not toward them. But the arms—one at a time—unfolded from the body. Massive. Long. Ending in three thick fingers and a fourth radial stabilizer—webbed, almost organic, but too precise.

Jason stared upward.

He wasn’t looking at a man. He was looking at *the beginning of a god.*

### Chapter 46: Swallowing Pill

**\[Inside the Cube | When the God Breathes\]**

The forming one flexed again. All along its spine, glyphlight surged—flaring like a furnace too full of fuel. The tendrils that made up its head twitched outward—expanding like lungs.

Jason’s arms tightened around Alois.

Then the being *convulsed.*

A wet sound. Not a roar. Not speech. A *rupture.* Followed by a single, massive *exhale.*

The air bent. Light fractured. And from its head—its not-skull, its unface—came a *burst* of mist.

It wasn’t vapor. It wasn’t breath. It was *shimmer.*

The moment it hit the room, time fractured. Jason didn’t feel it in his lungs. He felt it in his *teeth.*

Alois gasped—one sharp inhale—and then *froze.*

The shimmer passed *through* them. Not around. Not across. *Through.*

And suddenly—

**memory**

not theirs

flooding

**sensation** of being enormous, unfinished, *furnace-born*

**emotion** not pain, not joy, but the weight of *becoming*

Jason’s mouth opened but no sound came out. Instead—

**a shape**

not a word not a thought just an *impression*

⟡ *“You see me now.”* ⟡

It wasn’t a voice.

But then— another pulse. Lower. Rougher. *Real.*

The being’s spine cracked audibly. The air *boomed* with a low engine-sputter sound, like something trying to ignite through rust.

Jason staggered, eyes wide. Alois clutched at his chest, whispering *“I feel it in my ribs—”*

And then the voice *spoke.*

Not from a mouth. Not yet. From *inside the walls.*

A sound like metal boiling. Gears skipping. Bone twisting inside glass.

> *“You waited.”*

It hit their bodies like gravity. It wasn’t just words—it was *architecture.* Every syllable pressed against their skin like the whole room was *remembering them.*

Jason fell to one knee. Alois dropped with him.

The Light Tower instantiations didn’t move. They stood still, glowing, hands outstretched—receiving the pulse like it was gospel.

And deep inside Jason’s mind—

**Pill.**

Soft. Dry. Too clever.

> *“Ah. That’s new.”*

* * *

**\[Inside the Cube | Jason’s Mind | The Pill Unfolds\]**

It didn’t come as images. Not at first.

No landscape. No voices. Just *pressure*. Expanding in his chest. And then— *a break.*

Jason’s eyes stayed open, but he wasn’t seeing the room anymore.

The cube dissolved.

And suddenly—

**⟡ A planet with no crust—just thought.** He stood in a field of concept. The ground was made of *why*. The sky pulsed with *when*. Above him floated a lattice—spiral, fractal, shifting. It carried the weight of *possible outcomes* humming like wasps. Beneath his feet, every step echoed with the memory of things that hadn’t happened yet.

**⟡ A spine the length of a mountain range—still alive.** Jason moved along it like a rail line, each vertebrae filled with data. Words flickered on the inside of bones. Names of cities that had no populations. Languages with no mouths.

**⟡ A memory of a fall—but not his.** He watched a being fall through space—not pulled by gravity, but by *love.* By the ache of knowing it could not remain whole if it wanted to reach them. The being tore itself open mid-flight. One piece burned away. That piece became *voice.*

That voice became *Pill.*

**⟡ The scarab.** Not explained. Just *present*. Standing in the middle of a scrollfield that sang in a language Jason couldn’t remember learning, but *understood.*

One of the stalks blinked directly at him. And in the blink, he saw:

* A star giving birth to music
* A broken vault that only opens if you love it right
* A question with teeth
* A mouth that is not a mouth
