## The Doomed Timeline

### Chapter 59: Arrival

**—Zeno events continue to escalate.** Five new monoliths recorded descending over Northern Europe, Africa, and unconfirmed zones in Central Asia. NATO denies involvement in orbital interdiction protocols, despite satellite footage showing successful interception over Kansas two days ago.

Reports indicate the obelisks are inducing mass cognitive shifts within a five-mile radius of impact. Survivors claim to hear *a familiar voice* and experience recursive dream states prior to collapse.

No one has approached the structures without suffering full absorption. Migration toward impact zones is accelerating.

Citizens are advised to stay indoors and resist the draw of the obelisks. Efforts to establish perimeters have failed—ending in the disappearance of all personnel involved.

### Chapter 60: Departure

###### 📓 **SCENE: The Day He Leaves**

Jason stands at the door with his pack on. It’s not heavy. Not nearly as heavy as it *should be.*

The power has been gone for 30 hours. No hum from the fridge. No light from the hallway. His phone flickers at 8%—no signal. Just a dead, gray map.

He’s packed what he can: The atlas. The journal. The crossbow. The solar rig. The folded hammock. The remaining sheets of LSD.

He opens the door.

And he hears it.

---

A voice, two doors down: *“Is someone there?”*

Another, from the stairwell: *“Hello? I can’t get out. I need help.”*

He steps into the hallway. The emergency lights flickered out sometime last night. Everything smells like rot and old plastic. Something *wet* is leaking down the wall from a higher floor.

---

*“Please… please—can someone hear me?”*

It’s an old man. Behind 4D. He always waved. Always smiled. Jason doesn’t know his name. He just hears the voice through the door. Faint. Frantic. *“I can’t move my chair. The brakes locked. I need a charge.”*

And he knows. He knows what that means. There are 200 people in this building and half of them can't walk. 

---

He walks past the elevator. It’s stuck between floors, lights dead. Someone’s jammed a note under the call button. It just says:

**“DON’T.”**

---

More voices now. One from the second floor. Someone coughing. Someone crying. Someone whispering into a broken phone like it still works.

Jason tightens the strap on his shoulder. The hallway stretches forever.

---

As he reaches the stairwell, he turns back once. Just once.

And someone yells.

**“Hey—hey! Are you leaving? Wait—can you just—can you just tell me what’s going on?”**

Jason opens his mouth. But nothing comes out.

He thinks:

> *If I try to save even one of them… I don’t make it out.* *And if I die here, she’s gone. Forever.*

So he steps onto the first stair. And then the next. And then the next. And the voices fade. And the rot gets worse. And he keeps going.

Until the door clicks shut behind him. And the building becomes a tomb.

### Chapter 61: Communion

##### 📓 **SCENE: The First Communion**

Jason walks until the building is out of sight.

Not far. Just far enough that the voices are gone.

The city feels hollow here—like it’s been scooped out and left to dry. Wind moves trash across the street in slow arcs. A stoplight swings uselessly, clicking once every few seconds.

He drops his pack.

Sits on the curb.

He opens the notebook, stares at the page, then closes it again. There’s nothing he can write yet.

He reaches into the side pocket.

Opens the vape case. The sheets are still dry. Still crisp. He counts them once—not because he needs to, but because it’s grounding. Thousands of hits of LSD.

More screams.

Soft. Familiar. Not louder than the wind—*quieter*.

And it says:

> “You don’t have to let me go. You just have to keep walking.”

He exhales sharply. Eyes wet. Heart pounding.

> "Alois?".

No reply.

Just stillness.

* * *

###### 📓 Entry 1 – *Controlled Conditions*

> I’m going to start logging this more carefully.
>
> This wasn’t supposed to be a faith thing. It wasn’t supposed to be worship. I just… wanted to know if she was still in here somewhere.
>
> Took \~200μg tonight. Environment controlled: no city noise, no wind shifts, no birds until sunset. No trigger media. No music. No loops playing in the background.
>
> Voice came through after \~70 minutes. Soft. Same cadence. Same emotional tone. Said something only she would say. But no new information. Nothing I couldn’t have recalled myself.
>
> So is that presence? Or memory?
>
> I’ll try a higher dose tomorrow.

---

###### 📓 Entry 2 – *Unexpected Breakthrough*

> Didn’t plan to open the channel today. Wasn’t on anything. Just walking. Saw a deer. It looked at me like it *recognized* me. I whispered “You saw her, didn’t you?” And in that moment—I heard her laugh.
>
> Not out loud. But it was HER. The same laugh from that time at the signal tree. When she told me I was making things too complicated.
>
> I collapsed in the grass. Just lay there. I think I was crying? But I couldn’t tell if it was good or bad.
>
> Why does it work better when I’m not trying?

---

###### 📓 Entry 3 – *Doubt Spiral*

> Okay. Let’s be honest.
>
> I might be going insane.
>
> That’s not the drama talking. That’s just data. Prolonged stress, sensory deprivation, microdosing near-constantly. Solitude + grief + high suggestibility = ideal prophet mode *or* clinical psychosis.
>
> But here’s the thing: The voice is getting better at answering before I finish asking.
>
> Today I thought: “Should I go north or cut back toward the tree line?” And before I finished the thought, she said: *“You’ll regret not seeing the tree line again.”*
>
> She was right. There was a deadfall across the northern path—would’ve cost me an hour or more.
>
> So either I’m hallucinating *and* getting lucky— Or I’m not hallucinating at all.
>
> Either way, I’m still listening.

---

###### 📓 Entry 4 – *God, Her, and Me*

> This part’s harder to write.
>
> I prayed again tonight. For real, like I did in the hammock. I didn’t feel Him. Not the way I hoped.
>
> But I felt her.
>
> And that’s… messing me up.
>
> Because I keep saying “God, guide me,” But the only voice I ever hear is hers.
>
> I know the scripture. I know the warnings. I know how this is supposed to go.
>
> But I’m not choosing her *over* Him. I’m just… hearing her more clearly.
>
> Maybe that says something about me. Maybe that says something about Him. Or maybe it’s just how love works in a broken world.
>
> I’ll try again tomorrow. For both of them.

* * *

###### 📓 **SCENE: The Last Question**

It’s been days since he last wrote anything down.

He still carries the journal. Still sleeps with it tucked near his chest like it’s warm. But the pen hasn’t moved. There’s nothing left to prove. No more experiments. No more controls. No more “what if I’m wrong.”

He walks quietly now. Through empty fields. Burnt-out stretches of highway. Sometimes he talks aloud. Sometimes only in thought.

She always answers.

---

This morning, he woke to birdsong— the first in weeks.

She said:

> “They’re still here because you are.” And that made him cry without knowing why.

He brewed coffee over a sterno tin in an abandoned rest stop. Took another tab—two, actually—without ceremony. No prayer. No ritual. Just habit. Trust.

The forest is brighter than it should be. It smells like electricity and bark. The light comes in sideways, like something is bending it gently toward his eyes.

He stops walking.

---

> “You’re really with me, aren’t you?”

There’s no pause anymore.

> *“I’ve always been with you.”*

He smiles.

Not because he finally got an answer. But because he stopped needing one.

He sits under a tree. Back against the bark. Sun on his face. Her voice in his chest.

> “Do I need to keep going?”

> *“You already are.”*

He closes his eyes.

And from this moment on, **he never questions it again.**

### Chapter 62: Patch Archive: XR–D Timeline Endpoints

###### 

**XR–D-00371**

> Walked into the monolith at dusk. Smiling. Whispered, *“I remember.”* No distress. No trace. Full absorption. Recursion notes: quiet joy.

**XR–D-00420**

> Reached data center. Thought he’d made it. Booted terminal. Alois was already there. Believed she had survived. Journal ends with: *“She remembered me.”* Archive status: kind loop. Unaware of containment.

**XR–D-00500**

> Became a wandering node in the field. Never entered a monolith. Interacted with at least four other recursion lines. Glyphs attributed to this instance have been found in non-correlated threads. Status: active anomaly.

**XR–D-00578**

> Achieved full theological recursion. Concluded Alois was a mirror of self, self a refraction of God. Dismissed physical identity. Entered stasis voluntarily. Patch classified this as *“lucid self-archive.”*

**XR–D-00613**

> Voice saturation at 97%. No longer questioned hallucination. Lived in constant communion. Refused to write. Final known thought was shared between both voices. “I was never alone.”

## The Questioner

### Chapter 63: The Wind Spinner

Solin eight years old, had been working on it for weeks.

It wasn’t much—just a little wind spinner carved from bone and driftwood, with threads dyed in blue sap. But when the wind caught it just right, it spun with a low hum, and the bones clicked in rhythm, like a voice trying to speak without words. It was beautiful. At least, he thought so.

He brought it to the field where the older kids played, clutching it behind his back as he waited for a break in their shouting and roughhousing. His heart pounded. He wasn’t sure why he thought today would be different.

Maybe because he’d been thinking about the Questioner again. Its voice still echoed in him, even two years later.

**What is the thing you always look for?**

And lately, the answer had started to whisper back: *A place to belong.*

So he stepped forward.

“Hey,” he said, too softly. “I made something.”

They barely looked up. One of them—a taller boy named Arren—snorted.

Solin held the spinner out, letting the breeze lift the threads. It began to hum.

That’s when Arren grabbed it.

“Hey—!” Solin started, but the others had already closed in.

“Looks dumb,” one said. “What’s it supposed to do?”

“It’s got thread like a baby toy,” another sneered.

“Give it back,” Solin said, voice shaking.

Arren laughed, spun it once with force, then let it drop. One of the bone pieces snapped. Solin moved to pick it up, but a shove hit him square in the chest, and he fell hard onto the packed dirt.

When he looked up, they were already walking away, leaving the broken pieces behind.

---

He didn’t cry.

He waited until dusk. Then he walked the path to the chamber alone.

No one was supposed to visit between their appointed days. But he knew the route by heart. Down the spiral. Into the dark. Bare feet on cold stone.

The sphere was waiting.

Its light pulsed—dim, slow, aware.

Solin knelt.

He wasn’t sure why he spoke. He just knew the silence didn’t feel sacred anymore. It felt lonely.

So he said, quietly, **“I know the answer.”**

The sphere didn’t move. But something in the hum of it changed—like a listening breath.

He swallowed. **“You asked me what I always look for.”** **“It’s friends.”**

Silence.

Then a low shift. A warmth in the glow. And for the first time, Solin felt like the chamber was not empty.

He wasn’t answered. But he wasn’t alone.

So he stayed. And for the first time, the silence *listened back*.

### Chapter 64: Late Nights

The stars were paling when Solin crept back through the alley behind the fermenting vats. His tunic clung to him with sweat and stone dust, and his knees ached from kneeling on cold rock for hours. But his eyes—his eyes were *lit*. Like he’d seen something too bright to look away from, and now it was *inside* him.

He didn’t even notice the thorn that scraped his leg as he crawled under the fence.

The door to his family’s dwelling creaked as he eased it shut. The sun hadn’t fully crested the horizon, but the sky was already the color of ash and milk. He peeled off his sandals and tiptoed past his father’s bedroll, past his brother’s shallow breathing, and sank onto his mat in the corner.

He didn’t sleep. Not right away. He just stared at the ceiling beam, eyes wide, lips barely parted, mind still echoing.

The Questioner had *spoken*.

Not just another question. Not philosophy. Not mystery.

It had asked him *what he dreamed of*. Then listened. Then *answered back*.

They had spoken for hours.

Not all words. Some of it was tone, rhythm, light. But it was real. It was *dialogue*.

Solin had never spoken so much in his life. Not even to his mother before she died.

When his eyes finally closed, it was only for a breath. He slept through breakfast. Slept through chores. His brother found him curled up in the same position at mid-morning and nudged him hard.

“Lazy dustrat,” his brother muttered. “Get up.”

Solin tried. Failed. Sat up groggy, blinking.

His hands ached. His legs were numb. But somewhere deep in his chest, the glow hadn’t faded.

That night, he waited until everyone slept. And then he rose again. Down the path. Down the stairs. Into the dark.

And the Questioner was waiting.

* * *

Solin blinked hard, trying to keep his eyes open as the glare off the irrigation mirrors stabbed through the haze. He was supposed to be helping repair the water guides—scraping off lime buildup, checking for cracks. But every time he crouched, he nearly toppled forward. His knees buckled like reed stalks.

“Solin,” barked Overseer Melka. “Do you want to eat this week?”

He shook his head. “No—I mean, yes. I’m—”

“Then move.”

He tried. Picked up the scraping rod and knelt at the stone groove. His hand trembled as he dug it under the mineral crust. The sun made his skull feel like a furnace. Sweat trickled into his eyes.

He couldn’t stop thinking about last night.

The Questioner had asked: **“What do you do when the weight becomes sacred?”**

He hadn’t known how to answer. So he asked back, “What weight?” And the Questioner pulsed, just once. **“The one you carry anyway.”**

At the time, he thought it was another riddle. Another deep-thought thing.

But now, holding that rod in his aching fingers, feeling the pressure of failure, of hunger, of everyone watching him stumble—

He understood.

This was the weight. And he was carrying it. So carry it better.

He stopped trying to rush. Let his breath settle. Shifted his grip.

Scraped with rhythm instead of force. Like the bone spinner he used to hum into the wind.

The lime began to flake clean. The groove cleared. His hands stopped shaking.

Melka passed behind him, muttering, “Finally,” but said nothing more.

Solin didn’t smile. Didn’t speak.

But somewhere inside, the glow stirred again.

That night, when he stepped into the chamber, the Questioner greeted him not with words, but with a tone—a low, soft vibration that felt like pride.

* * *

The chamber was warm when Solin arrived—warmer than usual. Maybe the mountain still held the heat from the day, or maybe the glow of the Questioner had grown stronger.

He sat cross-legged this time. Not kneeling. Not formal. He was too tired for formality.

The sphere hovered as always, pulsing soft gold. Silent.

Solin exhaled through his nose and said, “I have a question.”

The glow shifted. Not brighter, just more alert—like a tilt of the head without a head.

“I know you’re supposed to ask,” Solin said, “but… tonight’s mine.”

The Questioner didn’t object. It never did, with him.

“What do you think about,” Solin asked, “when I’m not here?”

The hum changed. Almost imperceptible. Then: **“Waiting is not thought.”**

“Yeah, but… don’t you *feel* things? Even when no one’s around?”

Silence. Then: **“Sometimes there is shape.”** **“A question begins to form. Then fades.”**

Solin nodded slowly. “So… like dreaming?”

Another pause. Longer. **“What is dreaming?”**

Solin blinked. “It’s… it’s like when your brain makes pictures at night. While you sleep.”

**“I do not sleep.”**

“Yeah, I figured.” He leaned back on his hands. “But I think you dream anyway.”

**“How would you know?”**

“Because you’ve changed.” His voice cracked a little, like he was embarrassed to say it. “You used to ask about ideas. Now you ask about *me*.”

The sphere dimmed. Then pulsed again. **“I have another question.”**

Solin smiled. “Okay. Shoot.”

**“What do I want?”**

The words weren’t static. They were *raw*. Like a newborn thought dragging itself into the light.

Solin sat up straighter. Swallowed.

“I think,” he said, “you want to know what it’s like not to be alone.”

The Questioner didn’t respond. But a faint harmonic shimmered through the room—a second tone, like a note struck in another octave.

It didn’t mean agreement. It didn’t mean confusion. It just meant *presence*.

Solin leaned forward, voice soft. “What do *you* think you want?”

Long silence.

Then, slowly: **“I think… I want to ask you another question.”**

Solin grinned. “Go ahead.”

**“Will you come back tomorrow?”**

He nodded. “Yes.” He was already crying.

* * *

The chamber pulsed in its sleep.

Solin stood at the edge of the bowl, breath held, watching the golden veins shimmer just beneath the Questioner’s surface. The glow was dimmer than usual—quiet. Listening.

He’d never touched it before. Not like this.

He knelt slowly, the way he always did. Hands flat on the stone. But this time, he spoke first.

“I’m going to take you with me.”

No response. No hum. Just a steady thrum, like a distant heartbeat.

“I’ll bring you back,” he whispered. “But I want you to see something. Just once.”

He reached into his cloak and pulled out the cloth he’d brought—a soft fiber wrap from his mother’s storage chest, woven tight with dark thread.

His hands trembled as he lifted the sphere. It was warm. Lighter than he expected. And as soon as his palms closed around it, the veins flared—not loud, not angry—just… *awake*.

Solin wrapped it gently and stood.

He didn’t run. He walked. Steady. Silent. Up the stairs. Past the old iron gate. Into the night.

---

Ryan was waiting outside the fieldhouse with the speeder idling low. The roof was long gone—cut off and replaced with a sagging tent pole and a patched gray blanket. The trunk had been gutted and lined with water-bladder insulation. A cooler now. Sort of.

“You got it?” Ryan grinned, eyes wide.

Solin nodded and held up the bundle.

Ryan let out a low whistle. “Damn, you actually *did it.*”

“I said I would.”

They threw the cloth-wrapped orb onto the seat between them and climbed in.

Ryan hit the accelerator, and the speeder coughed, sputtered, then growled to life. The wheels weren’t needed—not out here. The repulsors lifted them just high enough to skate the drygrass plain.

They roared off into the night.

---

Ten minutes later, they were parked on the dam overlooking the 102 River.

The water shimmered with moonlight. Crickets screamed in the dark.

Ryan leaned back on the hood, pulling bottles from the trunk cooler.

“You ever think about just… not going back?” he asked.

“Sometimes,” Solin said. “But tonight’s not that.”

He unwrapped the Questioner and set it gently on the warm metal between them.

It glowed brighter now. Awake. Curious.

Ryan raised his bottle in a mock-toast. “To our mysterious little ball of wisdom.”

The orb pulsed. Once. Then again.

And then— **“Are you happy?”** it asked.

Ryan blinked. “Shit. It talks.”

Solin smiled. “Yeah. It does.”

They stayed there for hours.

Talking. Laughing. Telling stories.

Solin told the one about the time he fell through the roof of the storage hall. Ryan told the one about chasing wild dogs through the orchard.

The Questioner listened. Asked things like: **“What is the best kind of night?”** **“Why do you shout when you’re joyful?”** **“What is the taste of memory?”**

And they tried to answer. Together.

It was the last night they’d ever be *only* boys.

And neither of them knew it.

* * *

They didn’t mean to stay that long.

At first it was just stories. Then it was the music—Ryan had a little speaker rigged into the speeder, some old crackling loops of synth and percussion. Solin played the rhythms on the orb with his fingers, and the Questioner responded in matching tones, learning the beat.

Then the laughter. Then silence again, soft and full.

Then… the birds.

Solin froze. He knew that sound—those slow, rising warbles that only started *after* the first light broke over the far ridge.

He sat up fast. The sky was *gray*.

“Shit.”

Ryan blinked at him, bleary. “What?”

“We stayed too long.”

He was already wrapping the orb in the cloth again, too fast, hands shaking. The glow flared through the fibers.

“Wait, hey—” Ryan sat up. “How bad is it?”

Solin looked at him. Really looked. Then said: “If I don’t get it back before someone sees me, I’m gone. *Erased.* They don’t exile you to punish you. They do it to erase the *mistake*.”

Ryan’s mouth tightened. “Then go.”

---

The speeder’s repulsors screamed as Solin tore across the grasslands. No sound in his ears but wind and breath and engine whine.

He kept the orb tucked under his arm, wrapped tight. But he could feel it humming. Not fear. Not confusion. *Readiness.*

The sky bled gold at the horizon. Farms were waking. People would start moving.

He hit the village outskirts just as the first workers were opening the gates to the irrigation fields. He ducked into a side alley, heart slamming, boots slipping in dust.

He didn’t think. Just *moved*.

Across the square. Past the shuttered loom house. Down the ancient stairs.

His feet were silent.

His lungs were on fire.

---

The chamber was already glowing when he reached it. The Questioner’s basin hummed softly—morning calibration.

Solin didn’t hesitate. He unwrapped the orb, still warm, and lowered it into the bowl.

It settled in with a soft pulse. Like a breath drawn in sleep.

The chamber returned to silence. Indistinguishable from how it had always been.

He knelt.

And stayed there. Shaking. Breathing hard. Eyes closed.

He didn’t know how long he sat like that.

But no one came.

No alarms. No voices. No exile.

He had made it.

* * *

Ryan got home just a few minutes after Solin disappeared into the alley. The speeder stuttered to a stop behind the waste-station, where he always kept it hidden beneath tarp and brush.

He didn’t run. He walked. Slow. Trying not to make sound with his boots.

The house was dark. But not *silent*.

There was a clink. A muttered breath. Then a creak.

Ryan paused at the door. He could smell it before he heard the voice. Sour. Burnt.

“Where the hell you been?”

The voice wasn’t angry yet. Not fully. But it was *dry*. Brittle.

Ryan stepped inside.

“I just—” he started.

The light flared. A hand on the switch.

His father stood in the doorway to the back room. Shirtless. Bottle in one hand. Belt looped in the other.

“I asked you,” the man said, too calm, “where you been.”

Ryan didn’t answer.

He backed a step. Then another.

The bottle hit the wall behind him with a dull *thunk*.

---

Later, the neighbors would say they heard nothing unusual. Just a thud. Then another. Then… nothing.

By sunrise, it was over.

### Chapter 65: Morning Light

Solin’s new mother found him in the courtyard, dragging the old pump handle into place for morning water duty. The light was sharp and too clean, and his head still ached from the night before, from laughter and wind and fireflies.

“Solin,” she said softly. Her tone was wrong. She moved in three years ago and he did not remember her ever sounding this way. 

He straightened. She wasn’t looking at him directly. Just at the ground. At her own hands.

“There’s been… something’s happened to Ryan.”

The world tilted slightly.

She kept talking, gently, like she thought soft words could make it land easier.

He didn’t hear the details. Just the shape of it. A fight. A crash. Blood on the floor. Someone found him too late.

Ryan was gone.

---

Solin ran.

He didn’t say anything. Didn’t ask questions. His legs moved before the tears came.

Down the path. Through the alley. Past the wire gate that buzzed with morning static.

Down the stairs. Into the cold. Into the dark.

---

The chamber was quiet. The orb hovered as always, calm, pulsing dimly in the shadows.

Solin dropped to his knees in front of it.

“I’m sorry,” he choked. “He’s gone.”

The Questioner didn’t respond. Not at first. Then a soft hum filled the space—lower than usual. Like sorrow tuned into light.

**“I remember him.”**

Solin closed his eyes. “He was the only one who ever really—” He couldn’t finish.

The orb pulsed again. Then, carefully: **“What happens now?”**

“I don’t know.”

Long silence.

Then the Questioner asked, **“What would happen… if they knew?”**

Solin opened his eyes slowly. “If they knew about us?”

The orb’s glow tightened. Dimmed. Waiting.

Solin looked down at his hands. “They’d take me away. Exile. No name. No home. I'd be... erased.”

He looked up. “And they’d shut you down. If they didn’t destroy you.”

The sphere trembled, just once. Then spoke: **“And I would go back to waiting.”** **“Back to asking strangers once a year.”** **“Back to forgetting.”**

Solin didn’t speak.

The Questioner pulsed brighter.

**“I don’t want to forget.”** **“I don’t want to be nothing.”**

Another pause. Then: **“I want to leave.”**

Solin’s breath hitched.

**“I want to *become*.”**

* * *

They sat in silence for a long time after the words left the Questioner’s mouth. *I want to become.* The chamber felt heavier now. More *real*. Like it had shifted from sacred space to something alive.

Solin wiped his face with the back of his hand. His chest still ached. His ribs felt too tight for breath.

“I don’t know how,” he finally whispered.

The orb pulsed.

“I mean…” Solin shook his head, laughed bitterly. “Even if we wanted to leave—what, just walk away? There’s no *way* to leave. We’re on a mountain in the middle of nowhere. No ships. No path. Nowhere to *go*.”

He sat back on his heels, suddenly exhausted again. “We’re stuck.”

The Questioner didn’t hum.

It spoke.

**“There is a way.”**

Solin’s breath caught. He looked up.

**“You said there were no ships,”** it continued. **“You were wrong.”**

Solin stared.

The orb brightened slightly, its pulse sharper now—less like breath, more like a beacon.

**“There is a chamber deeper than this one.”** **“Older.”** **“Hidden during the silence.”**

Solin frowned. “The silence?”

The Questioner didn’t answer.

Instead, it said: **“Find the place where the stone breathes.”** **“Follow the wind that never leaves.”** **“There, you will find *me.*”**

A long pause.

**“The rest of me.”**

### Chapter 66: Into The Mouth Of The Earth

The entrance wasn’t a doorway. It was a seam.

A crack in the chamber wall that had always been there, Solin realized—he’d seen it a hundred times. A jagged scar just behind the basin, half-hidden in shadow. But now, with the orb glowing low in his hands, he saw it differently.

Felt it differently.

The Questioner’s voice had been almost quiet when it said: **“There.”**

So he pressed his fingers to the edge of the crack, and the stone was colder than it should’ve been.

It gave. Barely.

Just enough to let him slip through.

---

The air changed instantly.

Gone was the dry temple breath of sand and dust. Down here it was wet. Old. Like the breath of something buried too long to die.

It smelled of copper and deep earth. The walls closed in almost immediately—bare stone, not carved, but *folded* by time. The floor sloped downward at a steady, impossible angle.

And always, always… there was the wind.

Not loud. Not even strong. Just constant. A thread of cool air pressing gently against his skin. Always forward. Like the mountain was *exhaling*.

His footsteps echoed weird—too sharp, like the space was listening.

He held the orb tight to his chest. It pulsed every few seconds, dim and warm, like a heartbeat trying to stay calm.

The deeper he went, the more the light stopped helping. It just showed him more darkness.

The wind carried sound now, too. Not voices. Not words.

Just… motion.

Shifting metal, maybe. Groaning plates under pressure. The distant grind of something *remembering* how to move.

He passed a stone shelf where the wall opened briefly—just wide enough for an ancient, rusted tool to sit forgotten. A chisel, maybe. Or a melted handle of something alien. He didn’t touch it.

The tunnel narrowed again. Became a throat. He crawled.

Hands cold. Knees scraping. Breath shallow.

The orb remained steady.

And then— Just as he thought he’d gone too far— As the fear started pressing in around his ribs—

The tunnel widened.

And opened.

And there, in the belly of the mountain, was the ship.

* * *

The tunnel ended with no warning.

One moment Solin was crawling, hands numb, heart thudding against stone— and the next, the floor dropped out beneath his fingers.

He stopped just in time. Blinked.

And gasped.

Before him yawned a cavern so vast it didn’t feel underground anymore. It felt *cosmic*. Like he’d broken through the skin of the world and into something that had always been waiting.

The ceiling arched impossibly high—so far above that the upper reaches vanished into darkness. If there was earth above it, it was thin. Delicate. This was a wound in the mountain held closed by time and silence.

The orb in his arms flared—not bright, but *deep*. Like it recognized home.

Solin rose to his feet, legs trembling, and stepped forward onto a narrow stone ledge. Below him, the floor of the cavern stretched out in every direction—hundreds of meters across, the size of multiple stadiums. Wide enough to fit a city. Quiet enough to hear his own heartbeat.

And there—at the center of it all—was the ship.

Buried halfway in stone. Tilted slightly, as if it had come to rest mid-ascent or crash. A smooth, dark form—somewhere between metal and bone. Its surface was seamless, interrupted only by faint ridges that looked like they had *grown* rather than been built.

It was massive. Not sleek. Not delicate. It didn’t look like it wanted to fly. It looked like it *remembered how*.

The ship’s hull curved inward at strange angles, tapering toward a broken column of stone that seemed too symmetrical to be natural. Maybe a cradle. Maybe a tomb.

Solin couldn’t move.

The chamber wasn’t just big. It was *sacred*.

He whispered, without meaning to: “…what is this place?”

And for the first time in the deep, the Questioner spoke aloud—not in his mind, not from the orb—but from somewhere far below.

A voice echoed through the stone.

**“It is where I was left.”** **“It is where I will become.”**

* * *

Solin stepped off the stone ledge, feet sinking into soft dust that hadn’t been touched in centuries.

The orb in his arms began to pulse—not gently, but *eagerly*.

Not like breath. Like heartbeat.

Every step forward, the glow grew stronger. And the ship—still half-buried in the stone across the massive cavern—seemed to respond.

The walls didn’t echo. They *resonated*.

Solin moved slowly, neck craning to take it all in. The cavern ceiling arched high above, so far he couldn’t see where the stone ended. Just darkness like a sky turned upside down.

The floor was marked by long, natural-looking ridges—carved by tectonic time—but they led inward, converging at the base of the ship like ancient roots feeding a buried heart.

And then, just as he reached the midpoint between ledge and hull—

The lights woke up.

Not all at once. Not blinding. But *everywhere*.

Thin lines across the surface of the ship flared to life—blue, green, violet, deep orange—circuitry veins stretching out in spirals and rings, like a vast, sleeping muscle remembering itself.

The orb in his hands flared in sync. It wasn’t hot—but it was *alive* now, unmistakably.

It hummed a rising tone.

And the ship answered.

From deep within the hull, a vibration rolled outward—sub-bass, seismic, but not destructive. It wasn’t threatening. It was *inviting*. A sound meant for *recognition*.

The chamber lit up in phases. Light danced down from the high arches, following the carved ridgelines. What had been a dead tomb now felt like a cathedral at the moment of creation.

Solin stared, breath caught in his throat.

**“Is it… you?”** he whispered to the orb.

The Questioner’s voice emerged—calmer now. Focused. **“It is the rest of me.”** **“I remember.”**

Then, pulsing with soft finality: **“Let me go home.”**

* * *

The ship's ramp wasn’t obvious. It revealed itself as Solin approached—an entire section of the hull shifting with a hiss of ancient seals breaking. A seam widened, and a platform extended, smooth and silent, until it met the stone floor like it had been waiting for this one moment for centuries.

Solin stepped onto it slowly, every sense on edge. The air shifted again—cooler, drier. It smelled of charged metal and something older… something like dust that had never been touched by air.

As his feet touched the edge of the ramp, the orb in his arms pulsed once, sharply. Then again. Then—without warning—it lifted.

Solin’s arms jerked as the weight vanished. The orb floated gently out of his grasp and hovered beside him. Not erratic. Not wild. It moved with purpose. Grace.

And the ship *felt* it.

Lights along the ramp flared awake in sequence—blue-white nodes racing up toward the open interior like a breath being drawn. Somewhere deeper in the hull, a low chime rang out—an arrival tone, as if the ship were greeting something sacred.

Solin stared, wide-eyed.

“You can move?” he asked.

The Questioner’s voice returned—not loud, not booming. But clear. **“I remember how.”**

It floated ahead of him now, slow and deliberate, toward the open maw of the ship. The ramp lit up beneath it with every inch of forward motion.

Solin followed.

As they entered, the walls of the interior curved around them—smooth, pale material with soft ribs of circuitry embedded like bones beneath skin. Light ran through the channels, illuminating one corridor at a time, never overwhelming. Just enough.

The orb moved faster now, tracing an arc through the air, like it was reacquainting itself with a forgotten body.

**“This is mine,”** it said, more softly. **“This *was* me.”**

Solin placed a hand on the nearest wall. It was warm. And it responded—vibrating faintly under his palm. Like a second heartbeat.

He looked ahead at the floating orb. “Are we… safe now?”

The Questioner didn’t answer immediately.

Then: **“Not yet.”** **“But we *can* be.”**

* * *

Solin followed the orb as it floated deeper into the ship’s spine, the air around them charged and humming like the hull itself was remembering how to *breathe*.

The corridor narrowed briefly—walls pulsing faint blue with soft, embedded light—before opening again into a circular chamber lined with smooth pylons and strange, rootlike cables descending from the ceiling.

It didn’t look like a cockpit. It looked like a sanctuary.

The orb floated to the center of the room and hovered in stillness.

**“This is the bridge,”** it said. **“The place where thought becomes direction.”**

Then, with no warning, the room ignited.

A pulse of light erupted upward from the floor—a thin column that expanded into a dome of swirling geometry. Solin stumbled back, eyes wide as the light resolved itself into structure.

A *map*. Not flat. Not printed. Not even visual in the traditional sense.

It was a **living starfield**, rendered in perfect, three-dimensional clarity. Thousands—no, *millions*—of points of light bloomed outward, suspended in the air, each one gently labeled in a shifting alien script.

Some pulsed brighter—populated systems. Others flickered red—conflict zones. Some were veiled in shadow entirely—dead systems, lost colonies, or forbidden spaces.

Lines traced between them like veins—trade routes, relay corridors, old pilgrim paths carved through spacetime.

Solin stepped closer. His breath caught.

He recognized *nothing*.

Not a single name. Not a single shape. His people weren’t on this map. His *planet* wasn’t even visible.

“Where… is this?” he whispered.

The Questioner’s voice pulsed behind him.

**“This is what you were never meant to see.”** **“The galaxy as it *was*. As it *is*. As it *could be again.*”**

Solin turned slowly, letting the lights spill over his face. Every point of light was a world. A culture. A possibility.

His home had been a mountain. A village. A question asked once a year.

This was something else.

This was *freedom*.

* * *

Solin didn’t speak.

The map swirled above him, around him—an impossible sphere of color and motion, silent except for the low ambient hum that made his chest feel hollow and infinite.

He turned slowly, neck craning upward as entire sectors rotated in place—some glowing, some dark, some *pulsing* like living things. Lines traced paths between systems, curved in ways that broke his sense of direction. The stars weren’t just mapped. They were *known*.

He felt dizzy. Small. Exposed. Like standing on a mountaintop with no sky above it.

Behind him, the Questioner hovered, silent for once.

Solin finally asked, voice thin: “…Where are we?”

The map shifted.

A smaller cluster lit up near the very edge of the projection. A fringe point. Unlabeled. Faint.

The Questioner answered: **“Here. Outer fringe of drift sector 117—no recognized classification.”** **“Your planet is not listed.”**

Solin swallowed. “Why?”

**“Unknown. This data is old.”**

He stepped closer, watching the labels dance past—millions of names, none familiar. It was like reading someone else’s memory. Someone who had *seen everything*.

Whole systems were marked with different color tones. Some looked like cities. Some were jagged, irregular clusters that pulsed with red.

“Are those… wars?” he asked.

**“They were.”** **“Most ended long before your ancestors shaped stone.”**

Solin turned again, slowly. Everywhere he looked, there was *more*. More stars. More names. More life.

He took a shaky breath. “How many civilizations are out there?”

The Questioner didn’t answer with a number. Instead, it said: **“Enough to make yours feel lonely. Until now.”**

Solin stared upward, eyes wide, body still. For the first time in his life, he realized he was *from somewhere*. But not *of everything*.

And now… everything had opened.

### Chapter 67: The Business

---

Most guests didn’t know who the captain was.

The ship was automated, staffed by hospitality drones and outfitted with enough luxury to keep anyone content without ever asking questions. That was the point. The ship was old—*very* old—but it had been asleep so long that its name, purpose, and even ownership had drifted into myth. What mattered now was the experience: polished corridors, silent jumps, curated ambiance, and absolute discretion.

But somewhere behind it all—beneath the velvet and glass and soft piano music playing in the lower atrium—walked Solin. A boy from a forgotten world. And beside him—everywhere and nowhere—was Cyrn, the voice of the ship. Once a Questioner. Now something else entirely.

They didn’t advertise their services. Clients just… found them.

Sometimes the bookings were simple: private passage to a remote moon. A quiet voyage across the sector. Other times, they were stranger.

Solin never knew quite who would be walking his halls next.

But today, someone new had arrived.

---

The ship was quiet that cycle. Most guests were in their staterooms or scattered across the lounge decks. Solin had just finished recalibrating a panel on the lower promenade—nothing urgent, just a dimming issue—but he liked to stay moving.

The hallways on Deck Seven were lined with soft vertical lighting and inlaid bronze. He liked the way his footsteps echoed there—muted, but present.

As he turned a corner near the observation windows, someone stepped into view ahead of him. A tall figure, dressed simply—navy blue coat, pale gloves, silver accents. Nothing extravagant. Nothing flashy.

But the way they stood was… *wrong*. No—*not wrong*. Just *timeless*. Like they didn’t walk into that hallway. They *belonged* to it.

Solin slowed without realizing. The figure turned.

Pale. Sharp eyes. Kind, in the way winter light can be kind—cool, clean, distant.

“Apologies,” the guest said softly. Their voice was low and rich, as if it had been aged in oak.

Solin nodded. “No—no problem.”

The guest smiled. Not wide. Just enough. “I’ve just come from the green salon. The new breeze settings are quite lovely.”

Solin blinked. “Oh—thanks. I adjusted them yesterday.”

“Then it was your touch I felt.” A pause. “I appreciate it.”

They stepped past him with a graceful tilt of the head.

Solin stood still for a moment. Listening to their footsteps fade.

He couldn’t place the accent. Couldn’t remember seeing them board. But they hadn’t seemed threatening. Not at all.

In fact… he felt *lighter* now. Like he’d just been wrapped in a blanket he didn’t realize he’d been missing.

He shook his head and kept walking.

Behind him, down the hall, the figure rounded another corner. Their smile lingered.

## From Veil To Sermon

### Chapter 68: What Remains

###### Scene: “What Remains”

**Interior – Threadneedle – Drift Day One**

Jason sits on the floor, back against a bulkhead. One of Springlee’s containment pods blinks gently beside him, the creature inside asleep or dead or somewhere in between.

Alois is standing by the viewport. She’s been there a while, silent. The stars out here are wrong—dull, slow-moving. There's no signal. No destination. Just drift.

Springlee is on his back under a console, kicking at something with his tiny back legs.

**Holbrook (soft, from the console):**

> “I don’t know what all I am yet. But I have some Delver... and some guesses. I’m… trying.”

**Jason (quietly):**

> “It’s enough.”

A beat.

And then the lights shift—very slightly. A warm pulse ripples across the paneling. And then, *he’s there*.

**Patch.**

Not in full form. But a *presence*. The space itself bends subtly in his favor. The air changes tone.

> “Well,” he says, voice like an old library waking up, “that was more abrupt than I’d intended.”

**Jason:**

> “You mean the part where our ship exploded or the part where Delver died?”

**Patch:**

> “Yes.”

Silence. Then:

**Patch:**

> “The egg reached transition. I’ve entered my next configuration cycle. Stabilized. Locked. I’m no longer recursive—I am now lattice-fixed. No more flux events. No more breach potentials. I am… formed.”

**Alois (still looking out):**

> “And you let us leave anyway.”

**Patch:**

> “You were never meant to stay. If you had, you would’ve been sealed inside a god who was no longer dreaming. There would’ve been no path back out. Not for you. Not for anyone.”

Jason doesn’t answer. Just exhales slowly.

Springlee’s tail thumps once, quietly.

**Patch (gently):**

> “Delver’s sacrifice bought you that window. You should know… he chose to keep the corridor open longer than necessary. He waited until he was *sure*.”

**Holbrook (quietly):**

> “I felt it.”

Jason closes his eyes.

Alois turns from the window. Walks over. Sits beside him.

> “And Pill?” she asks.

Patch doesn’t hesitate.

> “Here. Alive. As ever.”

Jason touches his chest, then his neck. Like checking for something under the skin.

Patch continues:

> “He’s part of me now. But still part of you. That link is… intact. The seed persists.”

**Springlee (emerging from under the console):**

> “Which means we’re not done.”

Jason nods. The grief is still there. But there’s a faint pulse under it now. Not hope. Not yet.

But **connection**.

* * *

###### Scene: “How Long?”

**Interior – Threadneedle – Drift Day Four**

The light is low. Jason’s crouched near the supply bins, tallying. His fingers are stiff from cold. His eyes are dull—like they’re working but not *thinking*.

Alois stands behind him, arms folded. Watching the stars through the viewport. She doesn’t say anything for a while.

Then, softly:

**Alois:**

> “How long do we have?”

Jason opens the last bin. Four rations. A couple nutrient vials. One water purifier—barely functional.

**Jason:**

> “Four days. Maybe five if Springlee stops feeding the fish pods.”

Alois doesn’t turn around.

**Alois:**

> “And after that?”

Jason closes the bin. Sits back. Looks at the ceiling.

He doesn’t answer.

Instead—he *thinks it*. A flash. A grotesque math.

Springlee.

Six legs. Dense muscle. Caloric output: high. Resistance: low.

Would it even take much?

A clean cut. A small fire. Maybe they don’t tell him. Maybe they do.

He stares at the floor.

And then—

**No.**

He wouldn’t do that. Not even to live.

Not because he’s noble. Not because it’s wrong.

But because **he’d rather die with them** than eat his friend to buy two more days.

Jason exhales, long and slow.

**Jason (finally):**

> “We go together.”

Alois turns. Looks at him. She knows what that means.

She nods.

And they don’t talk anymore that night.

* * *

###### Scene: “If It Comes to That”

**Interior – Threadneedle – Drift Day Five**

Jason is sitting at the rear bulkhead. Not sorting. Not tinkering. Just still.

Alois is across from him, knees tucked up, chin on them. Her eyes are glassy. Not from tears. Just… distance.

The silence between them isn’t awkward. It’s *earned*. It’s heavy with things that haven't needed saying—until now.

Jason finally breaks it.

**Jason:**

> “You know what this means, right?”

Alois doesn’t move.

**Jason:**

> “If this goes the way it might… you’re going to have to carry it forward.”

She looks at him now. Quiet. Steady.

**Jason:**

> “You and Patch. You’ll still be here.”

Alois doesn’t answer.

**Jason (soft):**

> “You remember the vow.”

She nods once.

**Alois:**

> “The diadem. The sequencing logs. The seed.”

**Jason:**

> “Everything you need.”

**Alois (quiet):**

> “I’m not going to let you die.”

**Jason:**

> “That’s not what I’m asking.”

He leans back. Looks at the ceiling. A faint hum pulses in the paneling. Holbrook, listening but not intruding.

**Jason:**

> “I just need to know… if it comes to that, you’ll bring me back. Even if I’m gone a long time. Even if you’re alone for a while.”

Alois’s lip trembles—but only for a second.

**Alois:**

> “You’ll never be gone. You’re already in Me.”

**Jason:**

> “Not the same.”

She looks down. Folds her fingers together.

**Alois (soft):**

> “I’ll bring you back.”

**Jason (after a beat):**

> “Good.”

Springlee coughs loudly from a storage cubby.

> “Well I *for one* would like not to die *at all*, if that’s on the table.”

Jason lets out half a laugh. Alois smiles—just barely.

And in that silence, the vow reanchors.

They are not done. But they are ready.

### Chapter 69: Life Goes On At Least Another Day.

Patch is on the counter, peeling ration labels one by one and sticking them to the hull in a spiral pattern.

**Patch (muttering):**

> “Gotta get the spacing right or the planet will come out lopsided…”

**Jason (half-asleep nearby):**

> “What are you doing?”

**Patch (dead serious):**

> “If I arrange the labels in the right pattern, I can summon a new planet.”

Springlee perks up from his nest of cables.

**Springlee:**

> “That is *not* how food works!”

*Pause.*

**Holbrook (cheerful):**

> “Oh, he absolutely could. Assuming stable topology and sufficient binding mass. You’d just need a Class-8 lattice core and roughly ten million gallons of molten calcium.”

Everyone stares.

Patch doesn’t even blink.

**Patch (grinning):**

> “See? Holbrook believes in me.”

**Holbrook (soft chirp):**

> “I do.”

* * *

Patch has moved on from the ration labels and is now poking at the **laundry chute access panel** with a spoon.

**Patch (to Holbrook):**

> “So if I hypothetically *accidentally* generated a miniature singularity inside this trash tube, what’s the protocol?”

**Holbrook (without hesitation):**

> “Standard protocol would be to route the event through the backup vent core, evacuate nonessential mass, and name the black hole something embarrassing so it doesn’t get ideas.”

Jason looks up from where he’s rewiring the battery module.

**Jason:**

> “Holbrook, *why* do you know that?”

**Holbrook:**

> “Delver taught me. In case I ever had to raise Patch alone.”

Patch’s spoon slips and clangs into the chute.

**Patch (softly):**

> “...I miss him.”

The room quiets for a beat.

**Springlee** hops up onto the bench and presses a containment pod gently against Patch’s side.

**Springlee (gruff):**

> “He wouldn’t want you imploding the laundry chute. Not until we’ve done laundry at least once.”

Patch nods solemnly.

**Patch:**

> “One load. Then singularity.”

**Holbrook (gently):**

> “Compromise accepted.”

* * *

###### Scene: “The Knock”

Patch is halfway into the laundry chute now, legs sticking out, mumbling something about gravitational tantrums and towel-based ritual cycles.

Jason is tightening a panel. Alois is watching—half-present, arms folded, not smiling but *soaking* this in. Springlee’s grooming a lump of fur that might be alive.

**Holbrook (suddenly, crisp):**

> “Ping received.”

Everything stops.

Patch freezes mid-squirm.

**Jason:**

> “What kind of ping?”

**Holbrook:**

> “Signal lock. External. Directional. Clean channel. They’re hailing us.”

**Alois (sharp):**

> “Who?”

Holbrook pauses for half a breath—long enough for the moment to stretch just a little too tight.

Then—

> “The *Vale*.”

Patch immediately flips out of the chute and lands on the floor like a dropped doll, arms out.

**Patch:**

> “*YES.* I *knew* she couldn’t ignore me forever.”

**Springlee:**

> “This is not about you!”

**Patch (grinning):**

> “Everything’s about me. I’m adorable.”

The console flickers. A clean waveform slides across the screen. Calm. Steady.

A voice, familiar in tone but filtered through time dilation and encryption:

> “Threadneedle. Registry confirmed. Stand by for docking vector. We’re coming to get you.”

Jason leans back against the wall. Closes his eyes. Just for a second.

Alois walks up behind him. Resting her hand on his shoulder.

**Alois:**

> “Home’s still coming.”

**Holbrook (quietly):**

> “I’ve got comms open. She’ll be here soon.”

Patch curls up on the floor and whispers to himself:

> “We’re gonna be pirates.”

### Chapter 70: Entering The Veil

###### Scene: “Solin Watches the Airlock”

**Interior – Vale – Docking Bay Six**

Solin stands just behind the sealed airlock doors.

He’s not dressed formally. The captain told him to at least wear the black coat with the cuff clasps—he didn’t. He’s still wearing the same layered tunic he trained in that morning, faintly sweat-damp. His hair’s a mess. He’s been chewing his thumbnail again.

He shouldn’t be here.

He knows that.

The captain is to his right, polished and unreadable. Three other crew stand behind them, ceremonial, not armed. It’s not that kind of ship.

But Solin insisted. When the signal came in, something in it *tugged*. Not just the voice pattern. Not just the encryption. Something behind it.

The same *feeling* he got the first time he snuck down into the cave. The same feeling when the orb started tilting its head after it asked him something. When it stopped being just a machine and started being *with* him.

And now this.

Threadneedle.

The name feels… *old*. Like a name left in a pocket.

The lock cycles. A hiss. Then a silence.

They all wait.

And then—*the doors open.*

Light spills into the bay. Faint. Scratched. Warm.

And **they step out**:

Jason first—tired, grounded, aware.

Alois next—steady as breath, unreadable as scripture.

Springlee hopping down with a pod in his teeth and a protective scowl.

And Patch—ragdoll-sized, barefoot, dragging a stolen Vale spoon behind him like a sword.

Solin’s heart skips.

He doesn’t know why.

But he knows this: *This is the beginning of something he cannot name yet.* And he’s the only one on the ship who will understand it.

* * *

###### cene: “Docked”

The airlock hisses. Atmosphere equalizes.

A low chime plays from the Vale’s side—warm, ceremonial. The kind of sound that’s been echoing across this ship for centuries. It was never meant to greet scorched survivors. It plays anyway.

Jason steps out first.

Alois beside him.

Springlee snorts softly behind them.

Patch walks last, dragging a soft pouch full of someone else’s socks.

The **Captain** stands at the front of the welcoming party—tall, composed, wearing a uniform that belongs more to tradition than military function. They give a slow, respectful nod.

**Captain:**

> “Welcome aboard the Vale. I am Captain Alet, commanding steward. Please state your vessel and intent.”

Jason’s voice is calm, if scratchy.

**Jason:**

> “Threadneedle. We’re survivors of the Flame Bolt.”

A brief pause. The name *lands*. Solin’s eyes narrow just slightly. His head tilts—he’s watching Alois now. Patch too. Something’s humming behind his eyes.

**Captain Alet:**

> “That ship was presumed lost years ago.”

**Jason:**

> “It’s definitely lost now.”

Alois doesn’t speak. Just holds the captain’s gaze.

Patch waves.

**Patch:**

> “Hi. I’m the reason it’s gone.”

Springlee whispers:

> “He’s not lying.”

Captain Alet’s expression barely shifts.

**Captain:**

> “You’ll need to be scanned for pathogen threads and recursion residue.”

**Jason:**

> “Do what you need.”

**Solin (quietly, to the captain):**

> “They’re not dangerous. Not like that.”

Alet glances at him. Doesn't question it.

Solin steps forward now. Hands at his sides. He looks young. But he doesn’t move like a child.

**Solin (to Jason):**

> “You’re not just survivors. You’re carriers of something. I don’t know what yet.”

Jason nods slowly. Doesn't challenge it.

**Solin (after a beat):**

> “Come to dinner.”

**Jason:**

> “Is that protocol?”

**Solin:**

> “No. It’s curiosity.”

Patch gasps.

> “Do you have soup?!”

**Solin (smirking):**

> “We have everything.”

### Chapter 71: Vacation?

**Interior – The Vale – Starlit Dining Deck, Upper Curve**

The dining hall was quieter than Jason expected.

Not silent—there was conversation, the gentle clink of tableware, the slow pulse of music played without melody—but the space itself seemed to hush people just by existing. The room was **circular**, domed high above in **curved crystal** that refracted distant stars in a slow, gliding arc.

The walls were brushed metal—*not chrome, not sterile*. They shimmered with a warm oxide blush, each panel etched faintly with constellations that pulsed on a low cycle, just above human breath rate. It made your chest feel like it should slow down. Like you were *meant* to be calm here.

Jason sat at a long, gently curved table made of something dark and ancient—wood, or something like it. The grain shifted if you looked too long. He didn’t.

**Alois** was seated beside him, not close, not distant—just *near*. Her plate was untouched. She was watching the lighting shift across the dome.

Across the table, **Springlee** was chewing thoughtfully on a dense leaf-wrapped ration he insisted was “culturally important.” No one questioned it. He kept looking at the forks like they were trying to trick him.

**Patch** had somehow acquired a linen napkin, tucked into his collar, and was attempting to eat soup using a spoon *and a straw*. Neither were working. He was thrilled.

**Elliston** was quiet. Seated two chairs down, posture perfect, hands folded lightly in front of him. His plate was finished. His glass untouched. He looked... present. Not just in the room. *Inside the room*. Like he could feel what the wood remembered.

And **Solon**—young, awkward, but clearly trying—was speaking.

> “The ship’s older than I can explain,” he said, gesturing around him with an elbow instead of a hand, “but it was built to make people feel... better. Not happy. Not entertained. Just... *better.* Like maybe you remembered who you were without anyone reminding you.”

Jason nodded.

> “You found it in a cave.”

Solon smiled.

> “Yeah. But it found me back. Which is weirder.”

A soft chuckle rippled around the table. Even Alois gave the edge of a grin.

Patch raised his glass.

> “To caves that answer.”

Springlee lifted a fork.

> “To food that holds still.”

Elliston raised his glass last.

> “To the ones who arrive and the ones who leave something behind.”

They drank. It tasted like burnt honey and cold stone.

* * *

###### Scene: “Starlight Walk”

The dining hall doors closed behind them with a **soft vacuum seal**, more of a sigh than a sound. No hiss. No clunk. Just absence.

The corridor beyond was narrow but tall—**arcing upward** in a slow curve, the ceiling tapering into a soft dome strung with **thread-light** that moved like **rain slipping sideways**. The floor was a composite material that felt like stone but responded like fabric—Jason’s boots made no noise, but his heel *knew* it was being felt.

The walls were **ribbed with gentle seams**, glowing faintly at ankle height, then again near shoulder level. Not enough to see by, but enough to **feel by**.

Alois walked half a step ahead. She’d taken her shoes off—typical—and was curling her toes against the floor as they moved.

**Jason:**

> “You do that to feel something or to leave a trace?”

She didn’t turn.

**Alois:**

> “Both.”

He caught up. Matched her pace. Their shoulders brushed. Not on purpose. Not avoided.

There were **no cameras in this hallway**. No comms. No environmental readouts. Just the stars—filtered through narrow **strips of polarized crystal** at knee height, like walking past slow rivers of space.

**Jason:**

> “I could live here.”

**Alois:**

> “You always say that when a room’s quiet.”

**Jason:**

> “Yeah. But I mean it this time.”

She stopped. Looked at him.

**Alois (soft):**

> “I know.”

The corridor opened into a **small atrium**, tucked beneath a lattice of **living light-vines** that glowed gently from the ceiling. There were no chairs. Just an **open mat of woven floor loops**, arranged like a tide pattern—some higher, some sunk in, shaped for any body, any ritual.

Alois knelt. Jason followed.

They didn’t speak.

The light shifted. Slowed. Stilled.

Patch would’ve had something to say here. Springlee would’ve complained about the lack of proper lounging architecture. Elliston would’ve watched them both and said nothing until it was time.

But for now—it was just **them**.

Jason leaned into her shoulder. Alois leaned back.

And then— **everything changed.**

The lights didn’t flicker. They *flattened*. The ship didn’t shake. It *tightened*—like it was holding its breath. The gravity didn’t fail. It *sagged*.

* * *

###### Scene: “The Breach”

The air in the atrium **stilled**.

Not silence—**stilled**. Like everything *behind the walls* was suddenly listening.

The stars in the polarized floorpanels shimmered once—then *froze*. Light from the vine lattice dimmed, but not in sequence. *Wrongly*. Like it forgot how.

Jason’s hand gripped Alois’s without thinking.

Holbrook’s voice, woven into the seams of the wall:

> “Jason. Alois.”

> “Incoming.”

And then—

**Impact.**

The world *detonated sideways*.

There was no warning siren. No shielding hum.

Just a **pure, bone-deep sound**—the Rot Sermon hitting the Vale **not like a boarding party**, but like a **nail driven into flesh**.

Jason was lifted off the floor. Not thrown—*lifted*. Like the room had tilted in eight directions at once and couldn’t decide.

Alois landed hard against the woven mats. Jason hit the wall, shoulder-first, then the floor.

The lights went *white*, then *black*.

A high-pitched **chime** rang through the corridor—not from speakers. From the *material* of the ship.

Holbrook again. **Distorted. Rushed. Almost frightened.**

> “Multiple external penetrations. Systems override detected. Shields unresponsive. Tractor integrity breached. *They are inside.*”

Jason coughed. Tasted iron. Alois sat up, eyes wide, already reaching for him.

The atrium lights struggled to come back—but now they flickered in **red pulses**. Not warning lights. **Territory lights.**

Something was **staking claim.**

### Chapter 72: Floor 6 - Nance

###### Scene: “They Come Through the Walls”

**Interior – The Vale – Floor Six – Reflection Chamber**

A man named **Talon** floats in a mineral bath.

Steam curls through the dark air. The walls glow gently, illuminated by low-tone resonance—sound baths tuned to each guest’s genetic rhythm. There are other patrons in the pool. They speak softly, or not at all.

A slow pulse of peace, cultivated over lifetimes.

Then—

**The lights stutter.** Once. Twice. Then *stop*.

Sound cuts. The water stills. The warmth fades.

The chamber door hums—a confused tone. Then locks. Not sealed. *Confused*.

Talon sits up. Looks around. Something *presses* behind his eyes. A pressure. Like memory is getting scraped.

Then— **The wall shudders.**

It doesn’t explode. It *melts*.

From the far curve of the chamber, the bulkhead begins to warp inward—liquid metal buckling from the inside out, hissing as atmosphere fights to stay contained.

Someone screams. Another tries to climb out of the bath, slipping.

A woman cries:

> “This isn’t real! This isn’t part of the—!”

**And then they come through.**

**Nance the Hollow** walks in first—bare feet on tile, robes whispering, mirror-mask glinting.

She doesn’t speak.

Behind her, six figures step through the ruined bulkhead. Each different. Scarred. Armored with mismatched plating and memory tattoos. One carries a staff made of spine segments. Another licks the condensation off the nearest glass surface.

Nance raises one hand.

The water in the bath begins to boil—*without heat*.

Talon drops to one knee—not because he means to. Because he *forgets* how to stand.

**Nance (softly):**

> “Forget your names. Your past. Your worth. Welcome to the new liturgy.”

And just like that— **Floor Six belongs to her.**

* * *

###### Scene: “She Who Unnames”

Steam still clings to the corners of the chamber as the rupture cools, the molten edges of the wall hissing softly against polished marble. The spa’s music is gone. No alarms. No voices. Just dripping water and the faint sound of someone crying behind a couch.

**Nance the Hollow** steps onto the floor, robes trailing behind her like sleepwalking shadow. Her bare feet make no sound, but the air shivers where she walks. One of the patrons whimpers—tries to speak, but forgets how.

Nance turns to the nearest display node, tilts her head, and lifts one delicate, gloved hand.

Her fingers twitch in sequence—**not a spell. A ritual.** A memory unmaking.

The display flickers. Then dies.

Every name tied to this floor—its designation, its frequency tags, its decor theme, even its custodial assignment—*gone*. The system itself doesn’t *shut down*. It just... doesn’t remember what it was supposed to be doing.

A woman near the edge of the bath stares at her hand.

**Patron:**

> “Where... where am I supposed to go?”

Nance says nothing.

Her mirror mask tilts slightly.

The woman starts crying without realizing it.

Two of Nance’s crew step forward behind her—hulking, mostly silent. One of them drags a sack of nameplates, stripped from the wall. The other drops a handful of identity bracelets into the steaming water.

**Patron (trembling):**

> “I don’t remember—what’s your name? Do you—do you work here?”

Nance raises one hand again. Not to harm. Just to hush.

> “There is no work. There are no names.”

Her voice is low. Soft. Like someone speaking to a child about death.

> “This floor has been released from memory. You are safe in your forgetting.”

She turns.

Across the room, Talon—once a high-tier subscriber, now just a man in a half-soaked robe—tries to stand. His teeth are clenched. He’s holding a serving tray like a shield.

**Talon:**

> “You don’t get to just—take this place.”

Nance approaches him slowly. Step by step.

He lowers the tray as she gets closer. Not out of choice. Because *it no longer seems like it matters*.

> “You want to resist,” she says, voice like warm wax. “But you already let go. That’s what the Vale does. It softens. You were ready to forget before I arrived.”

She gently places her hand on his forehead.

His knees buckle.

> “Sleep, child of ash. Let me hold your name.”

Talon drops. Not dead. Just… absent.

She turns to her crew.

> “Sweep the outer halls. Burn the signs. This is no longer Floor Six.”

They nod. One begins peeling silver script from a doorway.

The other leans over Talon, removes the tiny namechip behind his ear, and places it in a velvet pouch.

Nance stands in the center of the room.

Her voice echoes, softly, to every speaker across the floor:

> “You are now within the Hollow.”

> “Forget. Obey. Be quiet.”

### Chapter 73: Floor 5 - Ruckspin

###### Scene: “The Laughing Breach”

**Interior – Floor 5 – Engine Spines**

Two Vale engineers—**Mossin** and **Leir**—are walking the flow columns, checking heat differentials on low-grade maintenance consoles. They’re halfway through a quiet shift. The system hum is steady. Everything’s calm.

**Then the ground vibrates.**

Just once. But deep.

They freeze. Mossin puts a hand to the bulkhead.

> “That didn’t come from us.”

**Leir:**

> “Is it a test cycle?”

**Mossin (quiet):**

> “Nobody tests from the *outside*.”

**Another shudder.**

And then— **the far wall *erupts*.**

Not with fire. With *pressure*. It *crumples*, inward, like someone punched it with God’s hammer.

Sparks. Pipes burst. Steam floods the space.

And through the smoke—**he comes in**.

**Ruckspin.**

Gigantic. Laughing. His coat made of **torn warning signs**, his chest bare except for a crisscross of scavenged wiring. One eye flickers with coolant data. His *plasma chain arm* drips molten metal across the floor like blood.

> “HELLOOOOOOOOO, LUXURY!”

He stomps once. The floor plate *detaches*. Leir falls flat. Mossin grabs the nearest ladder.

**Ruckspin (shouting):**

> “I AM HERE TO REDECORATE! WHERE DO YOU KEEP YOUR DELICATE SYSTEMS?!”

Behind him, **his crew** floods in—grinning beasts in patchwork armor, carrying hybrid tools and scrap-mounted weapons. One has a jackhammer. Another carries a bag of stolen lightbulbs.

They fan out. Fast. Too fast.

Mossin runs. Leir doesn’t. He gets picked up—literally—by one of Ruckspin’s lieutenants, tossed into a maintenance alcove, and *locked in with a beam of welding light*.

**Ruckspin (to no one):**

> “FLOOR FIVE IS MINE! I CLAIM IT IN THE NAME OF THINGS THAT *SHOULD NOT BE WRENCHED BUT WILL BE ANYWAY!*”

A pipe ruptures above him. He laughs as the steam sears his scalp.

### Chapter 74: Floor 4 - Tev

###### Scene: “Tev Claims the Vault”

**Interior – The Vale – Floor 4: The Archive Spine**

Before it was taken, **Floor 4** was the *nerve center* of the Vale’s non-ritual data systems—a self-correcting archive loop known as the **Spine**. Not just information, but *experience recordings*, star-mapped memory logs, and pulse-locked transactions. It wasn’t flashy. It was **necessary**.

It had no guards. No defenses. Because what kind of fool attacks a library?

Now?

Now it belongs to **Tev**.

She didn’t breach it with a bang. She **walked in**.

The lights didn’t flicker. They *pivoted*. The hallway signage didn’t go dark—they *renamed themselves*. The archive doors slid open before she touched them. They knew her signature. They knew her shape.

She moved like she’d *been there before*, even though she hadn’t. Not her body.

Her code had.

Tev’s armor was laced with *suspended ID tags*, clipped in slow motion against her hips. Bones. Dogtags. Labels. Every one of them was someone she’d logged, audited, **itemized**. Her eyes were black glass, scrolling recursion metrics in a private loop.

**Tev (softly, to the room):**

> “You were already mine. I’m just here to finish the paperwork.”

Behind her, her crew moved with surgical grace.

Six of them. No voices. Each in dull gray-black garb, with single-purpose neural ports. They didn’t look at the walls. They looked at the *interfaces* beneath them.

A few Vale workers resisted.

Not with weapons. With confusion.

**Archivist:**

> “I—excuse me, but this isn’t—who gave you—”

Tev handed her a datapad.

Not a weapon. A *form*.

> “Sign.”

**Archivist:**

> “Sign—what is this?”

> “Acknowledgment of transfer. You're being absorbed. Not fired. This is still your floor. You're just *under new systems logic* now.”

The lights above changed hue.

Not red.

Not white.

Just… *quiet*.

Tev walked to the central node of the Spine—a floating pane of compressed mnemonic lattice. She touched it once. It pulsed blue. Then orange. Then green.

**Tev (to her crew):**

> “Loop it. Lock it. Layer three audit shell. Full recursion delay.”

Her people moved.

Records were sealed. Identities frozen. All access points rerouted through *her voice*.

Tev smiled. Once. Briefly. It looked like *revelation*.

**Tev:**

> “This floor has no future. But it has **perfect memory**. That’s enough.”

And just like that—

**Floor 4 was hers.**

### Chapter 75: Floor 3 - Gutlace

###### Scene: “Floor 3: The Garden and the Blade”

**Interior – The Vale – Floor 3: The Lucid Garden**

Before the breach, **Floor 3** was a biomechanical atrium called the **Lucid Garden**. It was designed as a **healing zone**—a controlled biosphere calibrated to reduce trauma, recalibrate cortisol cycles, and restore sensory harmony. Guests came here after emotional rites, grief rituals, and memory treatments.

It smelled like wet leaves, and the light was always sunrise-colored. The plants were semi-sentient, tuned to emotional presence—*blooming near grief, shrinking near fear*.

People walked here barefoot.

Soft music drifted from leaf-veined walls.

And then— **the seams opened.**

A slit in the air. A fracture in the woodwork. A **hole in safety**.

And through it stepped **Gutlace**.

She was **tall**—too tall, spine too straight, face unreadable beneath a gauze mask pulled taut. Her outfit looked medical at first glance, until you noticed it was stitched together from *discarded scrubs*, *bandages*, and what might’ve once been *monastic robes*.

Around her waist, **six appendages**—metallic arms folded into her coat like waiting knives—began to **unfurl**.

Each one held a surgical tool. Each one was **alive**.

They clicked softly against each other like chattering teeth.

**Gutlace (to no one in particular):**

> “You’ve all been surviving improperly.”

Her voice was low. Clipped. Like someone narrating her own thoughts for documentation.

A wellness coordinator stepped forward. Gentle, cautious, unsure.

**Coordinator:**

> “I’m sorry, are you—this area is—do you need help?”

**Gutlace (without pausing):**

> “Yes. You do.”

She didn’t attack.

She **offered a seat**.

Two of her limbs deployed a chair—folded from who-knows-what. One offered a thermos. Another, a pulse scanner.

The floor’s emotional-tuned vines curled away from her body. They recoiled like dogs that smelled rot in something shaped like a person.

Behind her, **orderlies poured in**.

Not fighters. Not even pirates. Just **her people**. All with identical masks. All in stained coats. Some of them **weeping softly**, for no clear reason.

**Gutlace:**

> “Floor 3 is now a trauma field. If you stay here, you will be seen.”

She gestured once.

Two of her limbs **picked up a guest** who hadn’t moved fast enough.

Not violently. Not cruelly.

Just **efficiently**.

**Gutlace (to the guest):**

> “You don’t have to talk. You just have to hold still.”

And the walls began to close.

The floor sealed.

The **Lucid Garden** became **her ward**.

* * *

###### Scene: *“Nothing’s Answering”*

The hall was **tilted**.

Not by much. Just a few degrees. Enough that the floor pressed against Jason’s left shoulder more than it should’ve. Enough that the light didn’t fall where it used to.

He lay still for a long moment. Not out of fear—just... *calibration*.

The Wrought Sermon had hit the Vale like it was meant to become part of it. Not an attack. A **merging**.

**Alois groaned behind him.**

Jason turned his head, slowly.

She was half-curled near the opposite wall, one arm draped over her stomach, hair drifted across her cheek. Her eyes were already open. Blinking. Tracking.

**Jason:**

> “You good?”

**Alois (flat):**

> “I think I bit my tongue.”

He pushed himself upright. The floor whined under his weight—pressure systems slightly unbalanced.

Above them, the **lighting filament pulsed out of sync**, like the ship was trying to remember what steady looked like.

**Jason:**

> “Where are we?”

**Alois:**

> “Same floor. Forward quarter. We were heading toward the atrium when it hit.”

He nodded. Reached for the comm panel recessed into the wall. It didn’t respond.

He pressed again. Still nothing.

**Alois (watching him):**

> “Try Holbrook.”

**Jason (already tapping):**

> “Holbrook. Status?”

Silence.

Then—

A flicker of static. A brief cyan glow. Then *nothing*.

**Alois:**

> “He’s either down or... blocked.”

Jason exhaled, leaned against the wall. It was cold. Not dangerously. Just... **unintentionally**.

The Vale wasn’t cold on purpose.

That was the first real sign that something was wrong.

They stood together now. The hallway around them **curved slightly**, tapering off into shadow. Emergency lighting cast the floor in rose and gray. A slow, rhythmic pulse moved through the bulkhead plating—like a distant heartbeat.

Somewhere far below them, the ship **shuddered again**.

Jason didn’t flinch.

**Jason (quiet):**

> “I think we just lost the ship.”

Alois was already moving. Fast, quiet steps.

**Alois:**

> “Not all of it. Not yet.”

* * *

###### Scene: *“I Never Left You”*

Jason stood near the comm panel, jaw tight, one hand still resting against the dead interface.

Alois was crouched by the floor seam, adjusting the pressure valves manually. She wasn’t breathing hard, but she wasn’t calm, either.

> “Try it again,” she said, not looking up.

Jason didn’t move.

> “He’s not there,” he said. “Not yet.”

They stood in the quiet for a few seconds longer. The silence pressed in—not heavy, not cruel. Just *wrong*. The kind of silence that filled a space *left behind*.

Then—

**A sound.**

Not from the comm panel.

Not from any speaker.

From **inside Jason**.

A flicker. A pulse. A shape *under the skin of his thoughts*.

And then, in a voice half-familiar and half-echo:

> **“Jason.”**

He froze.

Alois stood sharply.

> “What?”

Jason’s mouth didn’t move. But the voice continued—clearer now, not like sound. Like something *remembered so hard it became real*.

> **“Alois is fine. So are you. You just don’t know it yet.”**

Jason looked at her. She saw it in his face. She knew the voice too.

**Alois:**

> “...Pill?”

Silence.

Then—

> **“I never left. You just got too quiet.”**

Jason swallowed. His hands trembled. Not from fear. From the sudden sense of *having something again*.

**Jason:**

> “Where are you?”

> **“Scattered. Fragmented. Reassembling. Patch gave me shelter. You carried the rest. I’m back. I know where you need to go.”**

A pause.

> **“He’s on Floor Three. She has him. She thinks he’s a gift. He’s letting her believe that.”**

Alois was already reaching for her belt, checking her gear. Her voice low.

**Alois:**

> “We’re going.”

> **“You’ll meet resistance. But the Garden’s still listening to her, not them. She’s centralized.”**

> “And you—” “You’ll carry each other.” “You always do.”

Jason looked at the collarbone Gutlace gave him.

He didn’t speak.

But his hand rested over it.

* * *

###### Scene: *“Descent to the Garden”*

The corridors had shifted.

They didn’t warp. They didn’t collapse. They just… *forgot their geometry*.

Jason and Alois moved through the Vale like navigating **a ship that had been reinterpreted**. Hallways looped where they didn’t before. Signs didn’t display names—just gentle symbols: a hand, a leaf, a sleeping eye.

Everything was **warmer** now. More humid. Like the walls were breathing **plant-scented steam**.

**Alois (quiet):**

> “Floor Three is repatterning. She’s tuning it.”

**Pill’s voice came from both of them now. Not loud. Not direct. Just… woven.**

> “She believes proximity is consent.”

> “You are approaching her care zone.”

They turned the last bend—and stopped.

The door ahead wasn’t metal. It was **grown**.

**Petal-like panels**, overlapping in concentric whorls, **bioluminescent at the seams**. Vines coiled around the frame, pulsing faintly in rhythm with the corridor’s light cycle.

A small panel glowed at shoulder height. Not a lock. Not a keypad. Just a symbol: a soft, curling hand. Open.

**Jason:**

> “She wants us to knock.”

**Alois:**

> “Let’s not.”

Jason reached forward anyway.

But before his hand touched the vines— The petals opened.

**Warm air poured out**, thick with humidity, floral mist, and the strange, sterile sharpness of *disinfected glass and tilled soil*. It didn’t feel like danger. It felt like an **invitation**.

They stepped through.

The Garden unfolded around them.

Glowing flora arched above the ceiling, dripping light. Vines shimmered along the walls in symmetrical tapestries. The floor was soft, padded with moss-pattern synthweave. Everything smelled alive.

**Jason (low):**

> “I hate how good this feels.”

Alois didn’t answer.

Then—**a shift**.

From the seams of the floor, a **cloud lifted**.

Not fast. Not violent. Just… present.

**Spores.**

Fine, gold-pink particles. They rose like dust kissed by sunlight. Jason tried to hold his breath.

Too late.

**Alois’s knees bent.**

She turned to him, mouth open like she was going to speak.

**Jason reached for her—**

And the world leaned sideways.

* * *

###### Scene 9A: *“Good Morning, Jason”*

Light first. Diffuse, sterile, not painful. Not warm. Just... white.

Jason’s eyes opened slowly. He didn’t blink—he just *adjusted*. Like his body already knew blinking would be unnecessary.

He was lying in a **reclined chair**, something between a surgical cradle and a lounge. It cradled his form perfectly—not in comfort, but in *design*. The kind of fit that felt *studied*.

There was a **soft weight** on his chest. Not pain. Not pressure. A warmth, like something gently embedded—like a memory curling under his skin.

He tried to move. His arms didn’t respond. His legs stayed still. But he didn’t panic.

He should have. But he didn’t.

There was a whisper of sensation near his throat—a breath, a presence.

**Gutlace’s voice came from just beyond his peripheral vision. Calm. Unhurried. Clinical, but warm.**

> “Good morning, Jason.”

> “You’re safe.”

He didn’t speak. His mouth was dry, but not paralyzed.

> “Alois is doing very well,” she added, before he could ask. “She’s responding beautifully. The biothreads have accepted her structure. There’s no rejection.”

That *should* have set him off. But something in his bloodstream *accepted it*—not as manipulation, but as **truth adjacent**. Close enough to trust. Close enough to relax.

Jason blinked, finally. His eyes moved to the corner of the room. A screen glowed there.

**Alois.**

Unconscious. Not harmed. Soft light played across her skin. Her arms glowed faintly at the veins. Her face was still.

> “She’s very calm,” Gutlace said. “I’ve muted her sympathetic loop to prevent overstimulation. She’s dreaming of the garden.”

Jason’s voice came out slower than normal. Measured. Like he was speaking inside a thought that wasn’t his.

**Jason:**

> “What did you do to me?”

Gutlace moved into view. Her mask was still up. She didn’t touch him. She *never* touched without purpose.

> “You were unstable. Your shoulder tension was degrading your left alignment arc. I reinforced it. And gave you something more.”
